FORM A BIG TRUST
CHICAGO, June 12.â 1t is announced the
gum- trust is now an actuality. The com
pany has been organized, the stock all
underwritten and the working details
completed. The concern has a capital
Stock of $9,000,000. Of this one-third is 6
per cent cumulative preferred. The per
manent officers will be chosen to-morrow.
All of the important plants of the country
are in the combine. Among the principal
factories forming the combination are:
Herman, "White, Tutti Frutti. Adams
Sons California Fruit Gum Company and
Kls-Me. The organization will be known
as the American chicle Company, chicle

MANY VICTIMS OF
THE STORM.
Number of Killed and In
jured Runs Up Into
the Hundreds.
BURKHARDT, Wis. f June 13.â A messenger from New Rich
mond has just arrived and telegraphed to Hudson for doctors and
help, saying a storm struck the town last night and a large number
of people were killed and wounded. The railroad tried to get out a
special train, but was unable to do so on account of washouts. All
the available doctors and many other persons are now driving across
the country to New Richmond, being piloted by the messenger. The
killed and injured are believed to number between 200 and 500.
STTI.LWATER. Minn.. June 12.â
This was a terrible nipht for New
Richmond, the beautiful little vil
lage being: almost wiped out of
existence by one of the most severe
cyclones that ever visited the locality.
It carried ruin and death in its path,
and at this hour it is impossible to
give even a partial list of those who
are dead or seriously injured.
It Is thought many are dead. News
of the disaster was brought here by J.
A. Carroll, a traveling: man from Port
age, Wis., who was stopping at the
Nicollet House in New Richmond when
the cyclone struck the town. He saw
the funnel-shaped cloud as it came up
the principal street, and he took refuge
in the basement of the hotel, which was
completely wrecked. together with
every other business house in the city.
In the terrible sheets of rain follow
ing the cyclone Mr. Carroll and his
comrades succeeded in rescuing the
proprietor, Charles McKennon, his wife
and <me child from the debris, and they
also removed a laundry- girl, who was
bo seriously injured it is not thought
she could possibly live. They also re
moved two men named Barrett and
Newell, who were very seriously in
jured.
Mr. Carroll drove to Stillwater by
team for relief, and a train left with
doctors at 12:15 o'clock. Carroll fur
ther says that fire followed the cyclone
and what is left is being consumed by
llames. Many people are doubtless
killed and the damage will run into
hundreds of thousands of dollars.

farm of F. C. Mattison. One edge of
the storm struck his buildings, twist

ing his windmill some rods. It then
struck the Gridley farm. The family,
seeing it coming, fled for -a large stone
quarry about twenty rods from the
house, where they remained safely un
til it passed. The house is a small
brick one and held to the foundation,
but the shingles were torn from the
roof. The cyclone veered to the north,
leaving the buildings unharmed but
tearing large oak trees three feet in
diameter out by the roots and twisting
them into every conceivable shape.

there it struck the bufTamss or ti. I.

not publish the paper. They carried

AN IDAHO GRAND
JURY SUMMONED
Judge Stewart Reviews the Circum
stances Leading Up to Mar
tial Law.
BY ASSOCIATED P^ESS.
WALLACE, Idaho, June 12.â
trials of some 300 prisoners who are ;
charged with complicity in the riots at
Wardner on April 29. when the Bunker
Hill and Sullivan Mill was blown up
with dynamite by a mob of 1000 min
ers and two men shot and killed, began
to-day in the State District Court,
Judge Stewart presiding.
The trouble which culminated in the
destruction of the Bunker Hill and Sul
livan property has been of long stand
ing. The miners' unions of the Coeur
d'Alene district have been very power
ful and for many years only union men
have been employed in the mines, ex
cepting the Bunker Hill and Sullivan
and the Last Chance mines at Ward
ner, which employed non-union men.
Great pressure had been brought to
bear, particularly on Bunker Hill and
Sullivan, and even threats had been
made to compel the owners to recognize i
the miners' union and to employ only j
union men. The Bunker Hill people
steadfastly refused to employ union
men.
On April 29 about 1000 men took pos
session of a train at Wallace and rode
to Wardner, where they blew up the
mill with dynamite, destroying prop
erty valued at $300,000. In order to pre
serve order and protect property, Gov
ernor Steunenberg of Idaho called upon !
me Federal Government lor troops, ana
within five days from the destruction
of the mill 500 United States troops
were in the Coeur d'Alene mining dis
trict under command of Brigadier Gen
eral Merrlam. Governor Steunenberg
declared martial law, which was en- j
forced by the Federal troops. In all
about 800 miners were arrested charged '
with complicity in the riot, but more
than half of these have been released.
Among those arrested were Sheriff
James D. Young of Shoehone County and !
County Commissioners Boyle and Stim
son.
The cases of the officials charged |
with aiding arid abetting the riot were
called in court to-day in the form of
impeachment proceedings.
The State was represented by Attor
ney General Hayes and Attorneys Bo
rah and Forney and the defense by
Hon. Patrick Reddy and Messrs. Jones
and Morphy.
Jones presented and argued a motion
to quash and strike from the records
the accusations against the defendants,
claiming that the citation to appear
had been issued in an illegal and irreg
ular manner.
This afternoon Judge Stewart grant
ed the motion to quash the indictment
against the County Commissioners,
Boyle and Stlmson, holding that Judge
Mayhew had no right to issue, a cita
tion to appear while holding court in
chambers. The prosecution then with
drew the information against Sheriff
Young. New informations were filed
in both cases. The statute requires five
days' notice to appear, so both cases
went over until next Monday with the
understanding that they might be
taken up earlier by consent of counsel.
The defense then endeavored to have
Young reinstated as Sheriff, but the
court would not hear them here.
The Grand Jury was then called and
Judge Stewart delivered his instruc
tions, which fill over eight typewritten
pages. He commented on the import
ance of their duty on the rights of all
men in court, stating that men have a
right to assemble or organize for law
ful purposes. Then he went minutely
into the law regarding conspiracy, riot,
unlawful assemblage, intimidation,
failure of officers to disperse riotous
gatherings, murder, destruction of
property and especially by explosives.

Judge Stewart said: "Your attention is

execution of process is frustrated and
defied by bodies of men and others;
that combinations of armed men resist
the execution of process and commit
deeds of violence.' "
Judge Stewart, continuing, said: "The
court is also advised through the press
and public rumor that on April 29 a
body of armed men came from Burke.
Mullan and other points in the county.
In charge of a train of cars, upon which
was carried gunpowder, or other ex
plosivea. proceeded toward Wardner.
where they were joined by others and
then proceeded to what is known as
the Bunker Hill and Sullivan mill,
where the same was exploded and great
damage done to property. John
Schmidt and James Cheyen were killed.
The truth or falsity of these matters
is especially referred to you for
thorough investigation."
The Judge then continued with an
explanation of the rights, privileges
and duties of grand jurors and their
relations to others. The Grand Jury
is made up almost exclusively of busi
ness irien from Wallace, Wardner and
Murray.

MANY VICTIMS
OF THE STORM
TnntinuM from First Page.
floor which covered the people in the
cellar. Not one of them was harmed.
Every piece of furniture was carried
f.r rods and literally torn into shreds.
< 'inside the house there were- two large
barns, machine sheds and outbuildings
and all were Oestroyed. Forty i-atue
and 100 sheep cannrjt be found at this
hour. W. J. Gilpin, who was passing
at the time, drove in there for shelter,
but was unable to reach the cellar. He
says It was not over two seconds after
the cyclone struck him before it was all
over and he found himself under a part
of a lumber wagon with timbers, horses
and machinery everywhere about him.
He was unhurt, but badly shaken up.
The storm passed on to the northeast,
destroying everything in its wake, and
was followed by rain in torrents and
terrible lightning. Telegraph and tele
phone wires are all blown down and
adjoining towns cannot be heard from.
HUNDREDS INJURED
AT NEW RICHMOND
MILWAUKEE. Wis., June 12.â A
message was received at the Wisconsin
Central offices here late to-night from
Stevens Point, from A. R. Home, divi
sion superintendent of the Wisconsin
Central at that place, as follows:
"It commenced to rain on the St.
Paul division about 7 o'clock this even- |
ing and the wires went down west of I
Jewett Mills aiout 7:30. At 8:45 we got
a message from Robertson, the opera
tor, via Marshall, that a cyclone struck
New Richmond about 7:30, killing and
wounding from 250 to 800 people.
"We have started a special out from
Irvine, leaving at 10 o'clock with sur
geons and to pick up what other sur
geons they can between there and New ,
Richmond. Also sent section men from
Jewett Mills with what help they can
carry on their cars. I will leave here
on a special as soon as I can get crew
out and will take all assistance I can
get in way of surgeons. Am trying to
get St. Paul to start a special from that
end."
A second message from Mr. Home
reads:
"Party just came to Jewett Mills and
reports that Dr. Ware was killed and
Dr. Epley has both legs broken and
that they are much in need of doctors.
This received at 10 o'clock. Don't you
think we should start a special from
Milwaukee with nurses and surgenos?"
TRAINS ARE BLOCKED
BY THE WASHOUTS
ST. PAUL, June 12.â Owing to wash
outs along their lines the various Chi
cago trains were held here to-night, it
being impossible to get through. The
Omaha is washed out near Hudson, the
Milwaukee and St. Paul has lost sev
frul bridges and considerable track
near Wabash, Minn., and in Wisconsin;
the Burlington is blocked by a washout
near Trempleau. Wis. The Wisconsin
Central line extends only beyond Still
water, Minn. There are also a number
|)f blocked places on the various branch
lines. The mail trains from the East
were unable to get through to-day,
owing to Sunday night's storm, and to
night's heavy rain and wind simply
increased the difficulties.
Locally the storm did much minor
damage. Over two inches of rain fell
in an hour's time and the wind blew a
gale.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, JUNE 13, 1899.
COUNTRY DANCE
ALMOST ENDS
IN A TRAGEDY
Miss Elsie Jewell Is
Badly Hurt.
KICKED BY A RUNAWAY HORSE
KNOCKED DOWN WHILE SAVING
A YOUNGER SISTER.
Accident Occurs at CorteMadera Early
Sunday Morning as the Pleas
ure Seekers Are Prepar
ing to Return.
While attempting to save her little fls
ter from being trampled to death by a
runaway horse Miss Elsie Jewell, one of
the most popular young belles of Lark-
spur, was herself knocked down and se-
v erely injured at Cnrte Madera early
' Sunday morning. The accident occurred
i at the close of a dance given for the pur
! pose of raising funds to build new side
walks at Larkspur. There were many
young people present from the surround
ing country and in the excitement attend-
I ing the htirrie<l preparations for depar
ture one of the animals became fright
en. '1 and. freeing itself by a sudden jerk
at the haltor. started on a mad gallop
down the road toward home.
There was a general scurrying to <?et
out of the way and the little Jewell girl
fell directly in the path of the frightened
animal. Miss Jewell saw her sister fall
and, ralizing her danger, ran to her res
cue. She had barely time to push the
child into a place of safety when the run
away was upon her. She was knocked
down and the flying hoofs of the horse
muv'K her on the head. Inflicting pain
ful wounds ami rendering her uncon
scious. Dr. Hopper of San Francisco,
who was in the party, did everything pos
sible for the injured girl and accompan
ied her to her home. Miss Jewell also
received several cuts and bruises about
the body, but it is not thought that any
of them will prove serious.
The runaway was not captured until it
reached San Rafael.
WRECKED PASSENGER CARS.
Three Boys Locked Up in the Tanks
for Robbery.
Willie Desmond, aged ,10, Victor Cher
main, aged 12, and John Bowles, IS years
of age, were arrested last evening by De
tective ODea and their names placed on
the small book at the City Prison. They
are charged with having broken into sev
eral passenger cars at Fourth and Town
send streets for the purpose of securing
the brass fixtures.
Within the >->ast few days four pas
senger coaches have been entered and
lamps and car seats wrecked, and the
police have been on the lookout for the
offenders. The arrests of last night were
the result. The boys, it is alleged, dis
posed of their spoil to a Junk dealer
named Symonds at Fourteenth and Har
rison streets, and to-day Symonds will be
arrested on a charge of having received
stolen goods.
The boys used skeleton keys and the
police are endeavoring to ascertain where
I they secured them.
CONSPIRACY IS ALLEGED.
Suit to Recover Mining Property
Worth Millions.
DENVER, June 12.â A suit was filed in
the T'nited States Circuit Court by John
T. .Jones of Los Angeles, Cal., adminis
trator of the estate of the late Nicholas
C. Creede, founder of the Colorado min
ing camp which oears his name, against
David H. Moffat, Walter S. Cheesman,
Sylvester T. Smith and L. E. Campbell,
all prominent mining men, and the Ame
thyst Mining Company, of which they are
sole Btockholders, to recover a third in
terest in the mine, alleged to be worth
over $5,000,000, and an accounting of its
operation since 1894. In the bill it is
charged that the defendants conspired to
induce Creede, who is said to have been
mentally unbalanced, to give them his
stock free of charge. The beneficiary of
the suit iB Dorothy Creede, the adopted
daughter of the dead miner, about four
years of age.
ARMOR FOR WARSHIPS.
Secretary Long to Purchase Enough
for Present Needs.
WASHINGTON. June 12.â The Ordnance
Bureau of the Navy Department Is pre
pnrjng to place a contract for diagonal
armor and a conning tower tube for the
battleship Ohio, now building in San
Francisco. The armor will be Harveyined
and will be purchased under special pro
vision of an act of Congress authorizing
the Secretary of the Navy to purchase
armor at the prevailing price if necessary.
He will be compelled to buy about thirty
tons. The Secretary will also purchase
about 538 tons of the armor for the Wy
oming, also building at the Union Iron
Works.
Rear Admiral Hlchborn to-day made
public reports he has received from naval
constructors, showing the progress made
on various ships of war now building.
The report shows that the Union Iron
Works, building the battleship Ohio, has
pushed the work of building to 5 per cent;
the monitor Wyoming to 8 per cent; the
torpedo boat destroyers Paul Jones, Perry
ana Prrble to 15 per cent.

WANTS TO MEET
JEFFRIES AGAIN
Fitzsimmons Is Looking
for Trouble.
MAY DEMAND RETURN MATCH
FOR THIS REASON HE WTLL NOT
ACCEPT A BENEFIT.
Noted Anatomist Pronounces the
New Champion the World's
Most Perfect Physical
Specimen.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, June 12.â 1n the area of
the Coney Island Sporting Club, In
which he won the championship of the
world from "Bob" Fitzsimmons. on
Friday night, James J. Jeffries to-night
boxed three rounds with "Jim" Daly,
his sparring partner. When the big
fellow climbed through the ropes and
seated himself in the corner which he
occupied when he fought Fitzsimmons,
5000 throats bellowed forth a welcome
that fairly shook the building. The
champion acknowledged the greeting
by bowing and smiling to all. He then
put on the gloves and sparred three
lively two-minute rounda, during which
he hit Daly'pretty hard, much to the
delight of the crowd.
Once or twice Daly landed on Jeffries
rather hard, and several individuals
shouted "Put him out, Daly." There
wore cries for a speech at the end of
the bout, and Jeffries, after consider
able urging, responded. Said he:
"Gentlemen, I thank you for your
kind applause, and I will do my best
to defend the championship."
"Bob" Fitzsimmons has declined to
accept Jeffries' offer of a benefit at the
Coney Island Sporting Club on Satur
day night. Martin Julian, manager of
the former champion, explained Fitz
simmons' declination as follows:
"Fitzsimmons is not in need of finan
cial assistance from any one at pres
ent; tht-n again, he is considering the
advisability of another match with
Jeffries. Were he to accept, a benefit
from Jeffries now it would place him in
a rather awkward position. In other
words, we do not think it would be con
sistent on Fitzsimmons' part to accept
a benefit from a man of whom he may
later demand a return match. FitEsim
mons appreciates the generous offer of
Jeffries, but he cannot accept it."
Jeffries has arranged to spar in the
following places during the week:
Hartford, to-morrow night; New Lon
don, Wednesday night; Providence,
Thursday night; Boston, Friday night.
One of the greatest anatomists in
America, or in the world, has examined
the new champion prizefighter. The
opportunity to bring the two men to
gether was afforded when Jeffries was
taken by hip manager to Philadelphia.
The anatomist who there examined
Jeffries Is Dr. T. Hollingsworth An
drews, who became well known the
country over at the time he was a
demonstrator of anatomy at the Jeffer
son Medical College, and who examined
more of the world's famous athletes
than any other surgeon In America. Of
comfortable means, he has retired from
active practice <>f his profession, though
the city of Philadelphia still retains
his services as surgeon of the Police
Department.
Every polieemiin on the Philadelphia
foree â and many of them are giants
has undergone a thorough physical ex
amination by Dr. Andrews. He has
scientifically examined 25,000 men. One
fad of the great anatomist for years
has been physical examination of the
world's great athletes, both in Europe
and America.
As a result of the examination he
has pronounced the new champion to
he to-day the most perfect physical
specimen In the â world. In the very
points where such men as John L.
Sullivan and Sandow have been weak,
Jeffries waÃ found to be strong. After
an examination lasting an hour, no
: physical defect was found.

FAIR KILLARNEY
TO BE PRESERVED
Sufficient Money in Sight to
Carry Out the ParK
Project.
NEW YORK, June 12.â The prospectus of a plan to buy the Muck
ross estate and the Lakes of Killarney by popular subscription by
Irish-Americans of Greater New York will be presented to-morrow to
those who have taken an active interest in the scheme. This Is pre
liminary to the public meeting at the Waldorf-Astoria Hotel on
Wednesday night.
James J. Coogan, James S. Coleman, John T. Fitzgerald and James
Boothley Roche, who are working energetically on the scheme, were in
conference yesterday and formulated the plan for the purchase and
maintenance of the park property, which will be submitted at the public
meeting.
The idea of purchasing historic old Muckross and the adjacent wat
ers of Killarney for an Irish National Park has met hearty approval
among Irish-Americans fn this city. Scores of them have signified their
intention to subscribe, and it is believed that after Wednesday night's
meeting enough money will be pledged.
Reports from Killarney, whither Richard C*roker has gone, speak of
the interest manifested by the residents of County Kerry at the pros
pect of Killarney becoming a national garden spot.
Many men prominent in Tammany Hall are enthusiastic over the
scheme, and it has been suggested that meetings be held in every
Assembly district in the city to help it. It was said to-day that the
poorer classes was anxious to subscribe to the purchase fund and were
only awaiting the result of Wednesday night's meeting to learn the
proper way to contribute and to whom their subscriptions shall be sent.

PROJECTED TUNNEL
UNDER THE OCEAN
Britons Favor the Scheme of Bring
ing England and Ireland Into
Closer Accord.
LONDON. June 12.â At a largely at
tended meeting of peers, commoners, en
gineers and others held this evening in
the House of Commons, a resolution was
adopted to address a petition to the First
Lord of the Treasury, Arthur J. Balfour
relative to the projected tunnel between
Ireland and England. The scheme is fa
vored on the ground that it would not
only unite England and Ireland more
closely, but tend to bring the United
States and the United Kingdom into more
Intimate accord.
The proposed route Is a distance of
twenty-five miles under eighty-five fath
oms of water, and the estimated cost of
the undertaking is Â£12,000.000 ($60,000,0u0).

CALIFORNIA SIGNAL
CORPS COME SOON
Cnntlnuf>d from First Page.
better dash and spirit and greater endur
ance. The volunteers flanked and charg
ed the insurgents over rice fields and
across rivers, and killed, captured or
routed them, while the policy of the reg
ulars was to waste ammunition and time
hy exchanging volleys with them from
Impregnable breastworks.
The superior effectiveness of the volun
teer fighting was so evident that the field
forces then forward have consisted of vol
unteers almost exclusively, while regi
ments of regulars lay idle in their bar
racks. It has been a war by volunteers
and of marvelous success. But neither
the "War Department nor General Otis
has recognized it in a public manner in
a way that might soothe the feelings of
the volunteers for the constant evidences
of distrust, sometimes amounting to con
tempt,' manifested toward them previous
to the opportunity they had of proving
their worth. From the rank and file of
the regulars the volunteers receive their
full measure of praise. They attribute the
superiority of the volunteers here to the
experiences of their Western life. Foreign
military experts on the ground write in
the frank admission that the best army
of its size in the world to-day is the small
body of volunteers which has accom
plished in three months what Spain did
not achieve in 300 years.
However, the volunteers do not make a
life business to engage in war. They are
of the opinion that it is high time for
the regular forces to relieve them and
take up the work which they make their
profession. No regiment of volunteers in
the Philippines has been consulted re
specting its wishes to remain longer in
the service. General Otis sent the erro
neous information of their desire to stay
on his own initiative, with the apparent
purpose of pacifying the public mind In
the United States.
If President McKinley will order a poll
taken In the volunteer regiments the re
sult will be that not twenty to the regi
ment will vote in favor of remaining
longer, now that the regulars have ar
rived in force and are doing practically
nothing.

PARIS, June 12.â M. Oharron said to
day: "I am very much surprised at
the turn the proposed international au
tomobile match has taken. It promised
at the start to be a serious affair, but
apparently it has stopped off suddenly.
"Mr. "SVinton issued a challenge for
any amount. I took him up. fixed the
stakes at 100,000 francs and accepted
a.ll the conditions made by Mr. Winton.
I paid over to the Herald 20,000 francs
as a forfeit.
"I do hope the American sprrrtsmen
will understand this difference between
the terms 'stakes' and 'forffeit,' as 20,000
francs forfeit cannot and never could
be twisted into meaning 'stakes.'
"Having placed this 20,000 tranrs mr
feit in the hands of the Herald, Mr.
Winton does in a like manner, deposit
ing his 20,000 francs in the office of
a New York newspaper. But this proves
beyond any question that Mr. Wintr-n.
accepted the conditions I laid down
and which were published immediately
in the Herald.
"New York newspapers which have
taken up the interests of Mr. Winton
so much in this matter will sureiy find
money enough through their readers,
among whom will he femnd persons
who have confidence enough in Mr.
Winton and who will complete the sum
in his behalf which is fixed in my let
ter published in the Herald. But tht
whole sum should be placed with the
Herald, where my forfeit now lies, and
where I shall pay in the stakes.
"I may say that b .t far as I am con
cerned 100,000 francs was found in twen
ty-four hours. Just as srmn as Mr.
Winton finds an equal amount I Will
pay over to the Herald the full sum of
100,000 francs. I hopp Mr. Winton on
his part will do likewise."
AUTOMOBILES FOR
SANTA CLARA COUNTY
Line to Be Placed in Operation for
the Transportation of
Passengers.
SAN JOSE, June Vl.â Automobiles prom
ise to be an Important factor in settling
the various portions of Santa Clara Coun
ty and making San Jose a business center.
L. Lion & Sons have large land interests
at San Martin, eighteen miles south of
here, and they are contemplating putting
on two automobiles between this city and
San Martin. The firm owns over 5000 acres
there and It is proposed to cut them in
small tracts. Two of the firm will go
East in a few days and while there will
investigate automobiles. If they should
prove a success on the San Martin route
more Will be purchased amt the aulo
transportation scheme extended in all di
rections, taking in Campbell. I-os Gatos,
Milpitas. Mayneld, Mountain View and
Palo Alto. The expense of operation is
slight and from fifteen to eighteen miles
an hour or a little more could be made
on the fine roads of Santa Clara County.
This would furnish quick and cheap
transportation. Thousands of visitors
â would be attracted to San Jose by the
introduction of these machines and as the
routes would run through the prettiest
orchard districts of the county it would
advertise the county's resources.

staring, his mouth open and his law

I who surrounded fiim. and made Dr. Gal
van, a stranger who had known Nelson

out bonds. There were, in fact, one sister
anil sixteen nephews and nieces living in :
the East.

the point of death at the time. The rela- !
tives had sent for an undertaker and hi?
had arrived before the will was made.
The Contestants propose to prove that the
relatives who were present were dissatis

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, JUNE 13, 1899.
THE PEACE CONFERENCE
AND PROBABLE CONFLICT
IN THE TRANSVAAL
Special Cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted, 1899, by James Gor
don Bennett.
THE HAGUE, June 12.â 1 ques
tioned a member of the Peace
Conference to-day as to what
would occur If during the confer-
ence England were to take up
arms against the Transvaal. His re
ply was:
"The question would be very difficult.
England would certainly contend that
I it -was her own business, the Transvaal
being a vassal and her action against
the Boers amounting to the simple
quelling of an internal insurrection or
rebellion. As, however, the Boers
maintain they are an Independent
power, the conference would certainly
I offer friendly intervention and propose
to act as arbiter."
The question is already being talked
'â  about in private conversation, the Eng
j lish delegates alleging that the Trans
i vaal matter is only a question between
! mother and child, but this would not
i ultimately prevent the Peace Confer-
I ence from believing it could not stand
aloof and witness a bloody struggle
silently without being ridiculous in the
eyes of the world."
1 had a long interview to-day with
| two eminent diplomats, members of
the Peace Conference. I gathered that
: the American proposal for rendering
\ private property inviolable at sea is
strongly supported by the Dutch dele
| gates and most of the other countries.
Germany, however, is indifferent.
! France strongly opposes it. She ap
pears to think that in case of war with
England, the possibility of which has
been shown by the Fashnda incident.
the American scheme would rob her of
i her principal weapon against England.
In case of a naval war it has always
' been the idea of the French to strike
1 on every pojnt of England's merchant
! marine and trade. They are not at all
willing to relinquish for the future
, what appears to them to be the best
chance against the nation that rules
the waves. It is quite certain also that
Russia supports the French view, es
pecially because she considers herself
bound in duty to back her ally.
The fate of the American proposal,
therefore, is very uncertain, although
Germany probably, in trie end, will be
found siding with the United States and
England, which may possibly turn the
scale.
At present the whole attention of the
conference is centered on the arbitra
tion question. It has falsely asserted
that the arbitration scheme, as dis
cussed, leaves arbitration optional. It
is made compulsory in twelve specified
cases, especially applying to interna
tional conflicts out of differences of con
tention regarding the meaning of treat
ies.
I have been able to read the Russian
proposition. It enacts that the con
ference will choose five powers to be
called upon to arbitrate. Each will ap
point a judge, five constituting a court,
to which two of each contending par
ties will be entitled to add a judge of
its own appointment with the same
right as the other Judges. A permanent
arbitration bureau, representing the
five original arbitrating powers will be
instituted, with headquarters at The
Hague, the expense to be borne by all
signatory powers in the same propor
tion as for the existing international
postofnee bureau.

Scenes of Wild Disorder

CLEVELAND, Ohio, June 12.â The at
tempt of the Big Consolidated Railroad
| Comparty to move its cars was to-day at
i tended by scenes of wild disorder In vari-
I mis parts of the city, though nobody was
j hurt and very little damage done to prop
erty. Cars were run on two lines at very
Irregular intervals, but without passen
: gers and mainly under the protection of
policemen.
The principal work of the mobs, which
\ were composed largely of idlers and spec
tators, was to blockade the tracks, In
which they were materially assisted, by
accommodating drivers of trucks, to cut
trolley wires and jeer at non-union men.
Occasionally stones and pieces of mud
were thrown, but nobody, was hurt. The
strikers discouraged violence, but they
', did must effective duty in inducing the
non-union men to quit, and In this they
were assisted by several women, relatives
of the strikers, who hoarded the cars and
pleaded with the new men to leave their
positions. One of these women was pro
vided with money, and when argument
failed she induced th^ non-union men to
quit by paying them.
Many of the cars were left on the lines
deserted and were afterward picked up
by inspectors and taken to the terminus.
: The company says it has sufficient men to
1 operate its "cars to-morrow, but this is
! denied by the strikers.
The Important development of the day
was the refusal of the company to let the
.State Board arbitrate the only question
Involvedâ the recognition of the union,
i The strikers say they feel greatly en
'â â  couraged and that they will be better pre
: pared than ever to resist the running of
cars when the attempt is resumed to
morrow morning.

i i;ir company has received \v<>rd from !

The dirt from which the $S was obtained
was dug and washed by the boy himself.
Some persons who have come back from
the placer fields, say that there is not -
enough gold there to work with profit. It
is generally believed here that those who
have brought back such reports are not c
miners of experience, but men who do not
know how to go at the work properly.
Again, some of them an 1 miners who
know their business but who do not want

ers to consign their dust to the com

amount for all that was shipped to
them.

ion, the reports of Pedro Rendon could
be taken with as much confidence as

Fp^flal Dispatch to The- Call.
SALINAS, Juno 12.â John B. Jones, a
well-known farmer residing in the south
ern portion of Monterey County, was shot
and killed yesterday by his brother-in
law, James O. <iill<tt. The weapon used
was a Winchester ntie, and the first re
ports of the affair that reached this city
were to the effect that Gillett killed his
victim when the latter was a quarter of a
mile away and was not aware that his
life was in danger. This the slayer de
nies. He pave himself up to the authori
ties yesterday, a Hoping that he had taken
the life of Jones in pelf-defense. The
facts regarding the killing have not yet
In en hrought to light, and the widow of
the victim seems disposed to. aid her sis
ter, the wife of the homicide, to shield
Gillett.
Gillett and Jones courted and married
two sisters, daughters of William Murley,
a pioneer of this county. It has heen
known for years that the two men were
hitter enemies, and the wife of Jones, to
avoid trouble with her brother-in-law,
persuaded her husband to leave the Park
lield district. They came to this section
and lived near Santa Rita. Later, how
ever, Jones moved to Tuolumne County,
where up to about three weeks ago he re
sided.
Moving back to Parkfield, Jones took
property adjoining the ranch of his
biother"-in-law and just across a creek.
The old feud which had slumbered broke
out afresh on Sunday. At some place in
the mountains the moa met to adjust their
difficulties amicably. They failed to ar
rive at a settlement, and once more parted
enemies. Jones rode homeward and Gil
lf-tt to his abode. The latter remained
only long enough to procure his ritle, and
then started out to Turd Jones. When the
two men met, according to the statement
s-f Gillett. the quarrel was renewed, and
to avoid being killed himself Gillett shot
fust. Jones fell from his horse and ex
pired almost immediately. When he saw
his relative fail Gillett went to Parkfield
and surrendered to Constable Sloan to
avoid possible violence from friends of
Jones.
T'pon reaching the scene of the murder
the officers could find no trace of any
weapons carried ny Jones, but It is
claimed they discovered a place in the
brush where there were evidences of some
one having been In hiding.
The whole affair Is creating no end of
excitement, and at the inquest to-morrow
and the preliminary examination on.
W ednesday it is expected there will be
ser.sationai developments. The friends of
Jor.es claim he was shot when riding un
armed up a lonely mountain trail, and
they threaten to avenge his death. The
families involved in the feud are among
the most prominent in Southern Monterey
County, where they have resided for many
years.

WAGING WAR ON
SUNDAY CONCERTS
Reform Attempted at
Sacramento.
CHURCH-GOERS ARE DIVIDED
MANY NOT IN SYMPATHY WITH
THE MOVE.
Bey. Mr. Miel of St. Paul's Declares
in Favor of the Sabbath Even
ing Recreation Acorded
thÂ« Populace.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, June 12.â For a number !
of years past one of the chief amusements
of the public of Sacramento during the
summer months has been the open-air j
concerts at the plaza, and the most pop- |
ular of these concerts have been those j
held on Sunday evenings. Now and then i
there has been expressed some opposition !
to these concerts on the part of certain
churches, but they were held from year
to year nevertheless, and continued to
grow steadily in public favor. Not until
this year has the antagonism assumed
importance, and there is prospect for j
some lively times before music in its j
voluptuous swell again floats through the ]
plaza palm branches while church bells i
are tinkling on the evening breeze.
Last night Rev. S. J. Carroll, pastor of j
the Sixth-street Methodist Church, dcliv- !
ered a sermon in which he denounced the
Sunday evening concerts as promoters of
immorality and as inimical to the
i churches. He protested against the ap
i propriatlon of public funds to aid them
and at the conclusion of his address a
series of resolutions were adopted by the
congregation calling upon the Board of
Trustees to refuse the use of the public
plaza for the concerts.
Stubborn as is the opposition on the part
of Rev. Mr. Carroll and his congregation,
it is not shared apparently by all the
| churchgoers of the city. Yesterday a peti
! tion was sent to each of the churches for
I the signatures of those naembers who
were willing to protest agaifst the Sunday
night concerts. St. Paul's Episcopal
Church is one of the largest and most in
fluential religious organizations in Sacra
mento. At three separate services yester
day the rector. Rev. C. L. Miel. presented
the petition, remarking that while per
sonally in favor of the Sunday evening
concerts, he believed that the members
of his congregation should have the op
portunity to express their individual
views. As a result of the presentation nf
the petition at these three services only
one signature was obtained.
To-night Rev. C. L. .Miel in an interview
I in the Bee asserts nis belief that the of
i fersea of which the opponents of the con
; certs complained could not be regulated
! by legislation or by other method than the
i exercise of parental authority. He asserts
that he has on several occasions, after his
r\ < ning service, attended the concerts at
the plaza and found there well-behaved
assemblages of people, who seemed to be
enjoying the music and the fresh air. Rev.
Mr. Miel gives utterances to these inter
esting remarks:
"I do not think that statute law should
ever bÂ° substituted for the great law of
fieedom of conscience. It may be, as
si.me predict, that this country is becom-
In a corrupt country, that its people are
becoming an indifferent. Godless people. I
do not share this view, but if it be true it
is not because its papers are printed on
Sunday; not because its boats and trains
are not locked in their docks or sheds by
order of the legislature on Sunday; not
IS4 the wnrkingman is allowed to
become for a few hoOrs a resting man on
Sunday, but because so many Christians
are selfish, narrow, little people; because
so many prefer pleasure to duty, and gain
to honor; btrcttuse co muny love their
party more than their church, their
church more than Christianity, their per
sonal opinions more than the truth, and
themselves more than all else besides.
"Those are the things that corrupt and
rot a nation. But the honest labor and
its reward and modicino, the proper,
wholesome recreation of its citizensâ these
arc not the things that corrupt or rot a
nation."
A delegation of local clergyman ap
peared before the City Trustees to-night
and urged that body not to appropriate
money for the Sunday concerts, nor t>> al
low the use of tlio Plaza for the purpose.
The board allowed th>- concert commit
tee to use the Plaza during the summer
months, without designating the days,
and appropriated $150 with the provision
that it should be usM only for mid
week concerts. This arrangement is
satisfactory to the committee, which ex
pects to raise enough money by popular
subscription for the Sunday night events.
LANDMANN'S SLAYER"
IS YET UNDISCOVERED
PARTICIPANTS IN THE MELEE
GIVE TESTIMONY.
Their Statements Conflict and the Po
lice Are in the Dark â Detective
Cody Seeks a Witness.
George F. True-worthy. James Mnnahan,
Frank Bush and William Sampson, the
young men who were engaged in the row
on Sunday evening which resulted in the
death of John Landmann, were taken be
fore the Chief of Police yesterday ami
each gave his version of the fight, as was
told in The Call of Monday morning.
Their statements conflicted, each prison
er making an effort to shield the other
from blame. The only Important state
ment made was that of young Monahan,
who still sticks to the assertion that he
gave the knife to "Hunkers" Trueworthy.
Trueworthy was shown the knife found
after the struggle and swore that he bad
never seen it before. The police kre of
the opinion that the weapon used in the
affray was secreted immediately after the
killing of Landmann. According to True
worthy, he and Rush were mixed up with
Landmann at different times during the
fight. It will therefore be impossible to
ascertain who was the knife-wielder.
Several other witnesses to the affair
were examined, but information of little
value was elicited. Detective Charles
Cody is energetically working on the
case. He is looking for a witness who
has not as yet come forward. The young
men are still in prison. They will ap
pear at the Coroner's inquest this morn
ing and may tell what they know of
Landmann's death.
LIGHTNING TRANSFORMATION.
Farmer John Hickey Meets a Bunko
Man and Now Wears Old Clothes.
Johy Hickey, a middle-aged man who
has just arrived from the country, ran
athwart a Clever bunko man yesterday
and last night he went to police head
quarters with his troubles. Hickey, who
had been drinking all morning, met a
stranger in a saloon at the corner of Clay
and Polk streets, and together they
started out to see the sights. The affable
stranger told Hickey he was a brother of
a prominent officer of the First California
Volunteers. They had many drinks to
gether and the liquor soon began to over
power Hickey. The stranger suggested
that he hire a room in a lodging house at
1522 Polk street.
Both men went to the room and when
Hickey woke up about 5:30 last evening
he found that the stranger had relieved
him of $45 and some small change and his
new suit- of clothes. The bunko man was
thoughtful enough to leave Hickey his
discarded suit, and as Hickey was a much
bigger man than the bunko-steerer, he
presented a sorry appearance when he
walked into police headquarters last
night to report the matter.

SOON TO START
FOR THE ISTHMUS
Canal Commission Not
Disposed to Delay.
ITS WORK TO BE HURRIED
WILL REPORT TO CONGRESS BY
JANUARY 1.
Civil Engineer Haupt to Lose His
Place Unless He Disavows
Interviews Credited
to Him.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
Call Headquarters, Wellington Hotel,
Washington, June 12.
Admiral Walker, chairman of the
new Nicaraguan Canal Commission,
has called a meeting of the commis
sion for next Thursday, June 15, when,
it Is expected, the question whether
the Commissioners shall proceed at
once to Central America and begin the
work in hand, or delay until the yel
low fever season has passed, will be
decided. It is believed the start from
New York will be made by July 1, or
before. It is said the commission will
endeavor to finish its work in time to
report to Congress by January 1 next.
Unless Civil Engineer Lewis Haupt
disavows the authenticity of published
views credited to him, his resignation
as a member of the Isthmian Commis
sion will be asked for and the vacancy
filled by the appointment of another
engineer. Notice has been taken by
the President of the interview with Mr.
Haupt, and it has been called to his
attention with the request that he at
once Inform the President of its cor
rectness.
This action is due to the earnest de
sire of the Chief Executive that a com
mission shall be organized which will
make a fair and impartial investigation
and submit an unbiased report. If Mr.
Haupt is already classed in favor of the
Nicaragua canal, as the statements at
tributed to him would seem to show
and so strongly opposed to the Panama
waterway, it is apparent that his re
tention on the commission, now that
his views are known to the adminis
tration and the public generally would
injure the value of its report. For this
reason the President has been con
strained To ask Mr. Haupt for an ex
planation.
The friends of Mr. Haupt here are
confident that the views attributed to
hirr. nre substantially correct, as it is
said he has talked in much ihe same
way to them. His removal from the
commission will mean a distinct loss
to the Nicaragua element, in view of
his Nicaraguan canal attitude.

MANIAC CHEESMAN
IS STILL AT LARGE
MR. AND MRS. FRED A. KXICK, I
bride and groom of a month, are .
spending the waning hours of j
their Honeymoon In the expecta
tion that it will be ended by the
bullet of the assassin's revolver. In
trenched in their apartments on the sec
ond f.oor of the house of Mrs. Elizabeth
Schultze, 909 Illinois street, they await his â¢
coming, the young wife in fear and
trembling, the husband brave but nervous, j
fully armed and ready to give a warm re- â 
etption to the Intruder.
Frank Cheesman, a bullet from whose
pistol left a scar on Mrs. Knick's body
Bhe will carry to her grave, is the enemy
on the trail of the newly wedded couple. ,
His record, which has been extensively
exploited; is a bloody one, full of murder
and attempts at murder, suicidal efforts
innumerable, commitments to insane asy
lums and subsequent escapes. The Knicks
have every reason to believe that he will
add tohis record another murder, that of j
thÂ« bride, who was Etta Payne of Oak
land, if he is not recaptured before he has !
the opportunity to slay.
The story of his appearance Sunday in
the*'Potrero. as published in yesterday's
Call, coupled with the knowledge that his
mania is only for blood, had the effect of .
causing intense excitement in that thickly- .
populated district. The prospect that the
madman might suddenly appear at any
home and kill the first one he met was not j
one to be ignored. Some few of the j
stronger-minded scouted the idea that j
Cheesman had been seen at all in the Po- |
trero, but his presence, revolver and all,
had been reported to Knickand his wife.
That they believed the report was shown
by their action in summoning the police
and in the fact that yesterday, while his
wife kept most of the time to a darkened
inner room, Knick, the handle of a re
volver showing plainly from his back i
pocket, stood guard inside the door lead- !
U.B from the hallway. 'â  - <â¢"*
Knlck plainly showed the tension on his
nerves caused by the responsibility newly i
devolved upon him of defending his men
aced bride and his rented castle from the :
approaches of the enemy. He believed
the report of Cheesman's presence in the
vicinity from an experience himself and
wife had had in the early hours of Satur
day morning.
"My wife was awakened," he said,
"about i o'clock Saturday morning by a
â  noise In the room adjoining our bedroom.
She aroused me and I listened until I
heard what sounded like some person
bumping against a chair. I got out of
bed in a hurry and started to go Into the
next room, which was- dark. I stopped
and picked up the lamp, which was
lighted, and took my revolver. Then i
pushed open the door suddenly and rushed
to the middle of the room to dodge any
plow that might be aimed at me.
"In an instant I saw there was no one
in the room, but I was convinced that
there had been from the noises both of us
had heard and from the fact that one of
the windows, both of which had been
closed when we retired, was up. I was a
little bit dazed for a moment at the idea
that some one had entered our rooms and
I went out of the window and onto the
piazza, but could Bee no trace of any one.
The door which opens from it to the hall
which communicates with the stairs,
leading to the street below, was unlocked
and I suspect now that whoever made the
early morning call upon us had gained
access to the window and made his es
cape that way. â _â -.-:
"As I as examining the door I saw
a man walking along the other side of
the street and I asked him to go to the
police station "ii Twentieth street and
notify the officers there that a man had
tried' to break into our place. Upon my
return to my wife, who had heard of
-man's escape from the asylum, she
insisted that he was the intruder. Since
I heard of his presence in this vicinity I

et and bath but for
shaving. Pears was
the inventor of shay-

During the afternoon Mrs. Knick and i
Mrs. Schultze stepped out to look at a

ing filled her with a desire to move. They
1 at the police Station and so in

their return both Knick and his bride

proceeding, the husband saying they felt
where they wore with his revolver
and a watchdog they had secured. If
they do intend to move, as the police were
informed, they wish to do so so secrety
that Cheesman will be in ignorance of
thi ir whereabouts for a long enough time
to allow of his recapture.
Lieutenant Anderson made an attempt
yesterday to find some trace of Cheesman,
but his men were unsuccessful, none of
them believing that the sanguinary luna
tic had been anywhere in the vicinity, ex
cept in the highly excitable imagination
of Mrs. Knick.
The entire police force of Oakland was
at work on the Belden mystery yesterday,
but not a single clew has been developed.
Although an organized lookout has been
kept for Cheesman, the police are not at
all sure he was the man who wielded the
ax in Mr. Beiden'a doorway on Saturday
night.
"We have not landed anything that can
solve that affair," said Detective Hodg
kins to-night. "It is a plausible theory
that Cheesman may be the man we want,
but I am not inclined to let it i-est at that.
There are many reasons why it might not
have been Cheesman. It hardly seems
reasonable that robbery could have been
the object, for no robber would attempt
such clumsy work. Had murder by a nor
mally minded man been the purpose it
would appear that he would have engaged
Mr P.elden In conversation and then have
â â  struck the blow that might have been
fatal. It certainly appears like the act of
a lunatic unless there is something be
hind It of which we are not aware."

Attorney General Ford argued that the
Federal "court had. no jurisdiction because
it was a mandamus proceeding and tne
Federal courts have- no jurisdiction ever
snch proceedings unless they he in , aia ,Â£ r
a jurisdiction previously acquired by tne
Federal courts. In this case the sole pur
pose waa to secure a writ of mandamus
commanding the defendant to forward the
package in question, and hence the writ
was an original writ and not in aid of
any other action or proceeding.
Counsel for defendant argued that the
facts disclosed by the petition did not
entitle the petitioner to a writ of man
date but rather to an injunction if any
thing and that the Federal court had
ample jurisdiction over injunction mat
ters. Counsel cited a long array of au
thorities, but failed to convince the court
of the correctness of his position. Ho
then asked for time to file a written' brief
and cite additional authorities, but to this
the Attorney General objected and the
court then remanded the cause to the
State court.

FREE CAMPING
Ideal camping spots all along the main
line, Sonoma and Guerneville branches of
the California Northwestern Railway
(lessee San Francisco and North Pacific
Railway), the picturesque route of Cali-
fornia. While parties can camp any-
where, close to or remote from the rail-
road, a few picturesque spots especially
adapted for campers have been selected
on account of their easy access to San
Francisco. -^
Glen Ellen Park and four miles along 1
Sonoma Creek, beautftully shaded. Excel-
lent fishing and bathing. Mr. C. E. Har-
vey, at Glen Ellen, will show locations.
Mirabel Park, on our Guernevillo
branch, adjoining the railroad, and skirted
by Mark "West Creek, which is now
dammed, affording fine bathing, boating
for two miles, and good fishing. Man in
charge. Row boats at reasonable rental.
Guerneville, in the midst of the redwood
country, and on the Russian River, is
practically the terminus of the Guerne-
ville branch. The people of the town and
vicinity, under the name of the Business
Men's Association, of Guernevllle, offer
free camping sites throughout the whole
section. The Russian River will ">e
dammed, affording a magnificent sheet of
water for bathing and boating. Mr. D.
Connell, proprietor of the livery stable at
Guerneville. representing the association,
will. meet all trains, and without charga
will show prospective campers the. various
locations.

of life.

dens were laid down with

were leaving by those who

Unknown for the woes of
the turmoil of existence.

GOOD-BT.
ALICE. HARRY
The pangs of misprized love, feeding
upon a disposition prone to melancholia,
drove Alice L. Miller, a domestic in the
employ of Mrs. Hannah Ross, to an early
suicidal death. Returning late Sunday
night after a day's surcease from work.
Miss Miller retired to her room at 426*4
Grove street, and ended her nineteen
y*ars of life by turning on a gas jet. She
was weary of the disappointments that
come from a one-sided love affair and de
libf-rately determined to end all.
Many letters written by the love
wracked girl and found in her room ex
pose the secret reason of her taking off.
The man back of it is Harry Kelly, a car
conductor on the Fillmore-street line, who
had known Miss Miller for the last six
months. About a month ago their friend
ship was brought to a sudden end. Since
The Fair Suicide and the Mm Who Rejected Her.
that time the deceased spoke repeatedly
of Â«the shallows of life and her desire to
â¢"fee herself from its burden.
"he body of the suicide was discovered
terday morning by Mrs. Ross. As she
a knocking at Miss Miller's door to
, aken her she smelled the heavy, nau
. eating odor of gas. Calling her husband
;;t the same time she entered the room.
Stretched out on the floor, her face rest
ing on the carpet, was the body of the
domestic whom Mrs. Rosa had Intended
10 summon to her work. The young girl
was completely dressed, with the excep
tion of her shoes, which were lying on the
floor. The tip of the gas jet had been re
moved and the gas wan buzzing full force
when the. Rosses entered the room.
It was a very late hour Sunday when
Miss Miller returned to her home. She
had been gone the entire day and the fact
that she was out after 11 o'clock was no
ticed by Mrs. Ross. At midnight Mrs.
Ross saw the light burning in her ser
vant's room and she went In there to
speak with her. The latter was sitting on
the edge of the bed. writing a letter. To
Mrs. Ross she made complaint of the bit
terness of life and said she did not care to j
live. She burst into tears and confessed I
that it was all over with her hopeful af- i
fection for Harry Kelly. According to her j
story she had met Kelly ttiat afternoon
and â â .ilkrd â with him on the street. On
her l<-rom she wore a pin m which was
lilfi picture. He asked her for it, but she
refused to give it up. He obtained mok
session of the picture, however, and it Is I
this incident which she refers to in the
last letter she wrote. It is as follows:
Dear Harry: I parted with you. but you |
know that i said I would not part with :
your picture only In death
You can blame yourself for the Rood-by.
You have no right to take that pin.
Harry Kelly, when seen yesterday, was
at his post, tallying fares. "She always
said she- didn't care to live," he said. "I
hav^ known her about six months, but
have had nothing to do with her for the
lrst month or more. I feared ' that she
| was not thoroughly sane, so 1 left her.
Fes, I suppose she was In love with me."
Kelly admits that he met the deceased a

LIFE HELD NO
JOYS FOR HER.
OAKLAND, June 12.â After frequent
threats that she would commit suicide
and while in a fit of despondency over im
apinary love troubles. Miss Alien Marquet.
aped 19 years, this morning swallowed two
ounces of carbolic acid to make good her
threats and end her troubles.
The young woman had been residing at
the residence of her uncle, Captain .1
Morrison, 65S Jackson street, acting as his
housekeeper, for the past six years, while
her mother. Mrs. Campania. lives at
Temescal. She had not been in tin- best
of health for the past few days, suffering
considerably from headaches.
Shortly before S o'clock Miss Marquet
left her home to secure postage stamps
for two letters she had just previously
written. One of these letters whs ad
dressed to Harry Troy, a saloon-keeper
on Battery street. San Francisco, with
whom she had been keeping company. It
is presumed that she also secured the
poison at a neighboring drugstore and on
returning to her homo she. Immediately
quaffed the deadly stuff and sank down
on the bark stairs, where her groans at
tracted the attention of her friend, Miss
Mildred Herspring of 21ft Second street.
who had just called. Horror-stricken,
Herspring asked her friend what
sh.e had dune. "This Is my last act "
answered the dying young woman
"Please send for mother." Then her head
dropped and she became unconscious.
Miss Marquet was promptly removed to
the Reviving Hospital, where Dr. Strat
lon and Steward Borchert applied the
stomach pump and relieved her of about
two ounce? of pure carbolic acid. Anti
dotes were immediately applied and dur
ing the afternoon the hospital physician
expressed some hopes that her life' might
be saved. Her lips, mouth and throat are
terriMy burned.
It is supposed by some that Miss Mar
rjuet's rash act was prompted through
disappointment in a love affair. Neigh
bors, however, believe that nervous head
aches nearly drove her marl mid that she
did not know what she whs doing. Yet
according to a statement by her Bister!
who called at the luispit.il later in the
day. Alice had frequently threatened to
end her life.
A man. supposed to be Harry Troy but
who emphatically declined to disclose his
name, also railed at the hospital. He
stated that he .had been in the girls com
pany on Sunday afternoon and at tlmt
time she was in pood spirits. "We had no
quarrels that 1 can think of," said the
stranger, "but Alice said a week ago that
Bhe Was tired of life and would sonic day
take a dose that would kill her. She
Claimed she had been disappointed once.
but 1 did not learn what about. Her uncle
always treated her kindlyâ more 80, In
fact, than did her parents."
Mrs. Campania the girl's mother, la
said to be a laundress and her stepfather
a traveling tinsmith.

condltlou Improved slightly and the physi

GENERAL YOUNG ARRIVES.
Won Distinction for Deeds of Valor in
the War.
Major General S. M. B. Young and staff
arrived in this city yesterday morning on
the Portland train. They came from the
East and will depart for Manila on the
next transport. General Young com
manded the Second Cavalry Brigade of
Wheeler's division at Santiago and fought
i.i the battle at Las Quasimas. His last
command was of the Second Army Corps

Hurt in a Collision.
John Adams, a well-known builder and
contractor, residing at 716 Haighf street.
was injured shortly after 6 o'clock last
evening in a collision between his buggy
and that of A. Straaaer, a dealer in poul
try. The accident occurred at the corner
of Polk street and Golden Gate avenue.
The Impact of tiv vehicles was so sudden
that Mr. Adams was thrown to the ground
and sustained injuries that necessitated
sending him to the hospital. On his ar
rival tlvre the doctor in charge found a
nasty scalp wound. The buggy was dam
:i. Ed in the collision.
Missionites Hold a Meeting.
At a meeting of the Mission business
men last night it was decided to call a
mass meeting at an early late for the
purpose of obtaining an expression on the
repaying of Valencia street with bitumen
from Market to Twenty-sixth streets.
'In make final arrangements for this
mass meeting a committee consisting of
Secretary E. S. Harrison. G. Golden and
Eugene McCoy was appointed with full
power to act.
It is the intention of the committee to
invite Mayor Phelan and F. W. I>ohr
mann, president of the Merchants" Asso
ciation, to the meeting and have them to
make short addresses pointing out the
necessity of repaying the street.
The vacancy in the presidency was filled
by the election of Fred Wai'bel. aitd a
second vice president was added by elect
ing W. A. C. Smith. This leaves the as
sociation with a president and two vice
presidents, Eugene McCoy having been
elected tirst vice president at the organi
zation of the board of directors.
Neill and McConnell Matched.
Al Neill ami Frank McConnell have \
been matched to box twenty rounds be
fore the National Club July 26. The men
boxed a draw some months ago. Since
then Neill has defeated Tom Treacy and
OJeorge Green, while McConnell secured
a decision over Billy Gallagher in Los I
Angeles. The men have been guaranteed
8 purse of 13500. Both pugilists have a |
strong following here, so the match is
expected to prove an attractive one.

ENDS LIFE
BY POISON
Susie Hauck Takes
Carbolic Acid
and Dies.
MRS. ST'RIE HAUCK, a dressmaker
about 35 years old, committed sui
cide by swallowing a dose of car
bolic acid some time yesterday at
25] Octavia street. She was alone in tht
house at the time and the fact of the sui
cide was not known until about 8 o'clock
last ni?ht, when T. F. Holz, a roomer in
the house, returned and found the body
of his landlady half reclining on a little
cot in a rear room. A strong odor of acid
pervaded the apartm?nt and a wineglass
lying on the floor by the side of the cot
still held a drop or two of the poison. A
two-ounce bottle containing half an ounce
of the acid stood on the sewing machine
near by.
On a table in the kitchen were a few
fragments of a letter which the woman
had written and then destroyed. The let
ter was unfinished. It read:
"Love, do not feel broken-hearted. Mv
father died for me and I die for my boy."
An envelope had also been addressed -to
Mrs. Eisett, Hayes street, near Lagunu,
and then destroyed.
The dead woman was the wife of Peter
Haiick, an ex-policeman, but the couple
separated about a year ago and since that
time Mrs. Haucfc has been earning a liv
ing for herself and her 13-year-old bo.y
working as a domestic. About three
weeks ago Holz, who had known her for
some time, furnished the Octavla-street
house for her and she started in business
as a dressmaker, yesterday morning i-hc
was feeling downcast and told Holz i hut
when she thought of her past she felt like
taking her life. He urged her not to gel
discouraged, and wh>n h>' left her she ap
peared to have recovered her spirits arid
he had no idea she would put her implied
threat into execution. But she had evi
dently begun preparations to put an end
to her troubles almost immediately, for
when he nHurncd at night she had been
dead for several hours.
Holz at once notified the police and the
Coroner's office and the body was remov
ed to the Morgue. BTolz was taken to the
City Prison and detained as a witness.
Mrs. Hauck is said to have been well con
nected in the Bast, but, so far as known,
had no relatives on the coast. Her son
is boarding with friends in this city. The
inquest will be held to-day.

FOR, LICENSE COLLECTOR.
Commissioners Meet and Take Three
Ballots, but Fail to Make a
Selection.
Mayor Fhelan, Auditor "Wells and
Treasurer Truman mot yesterday morn
ins for the purpose of electing a License
Collector to succeed Theodore F. Bonnet,
the incumbent; but after taking
three ballots without arriving at a choice
they adjourned to meet again to-day.
Auditor Wells voted for William Z. Tiff
any, Treasurer Truman cast his ballot for
Frederick W. Lees and the Mayor voted
for Bonnet.
It is expected that at the meeting to
day Lees will be elected, as it is known
that he has received assurances that his
election would be confirmed by all the
Republican members of the Board of Su
pervisors. So far Auditor Wells has
kept his promise to the public that ho
would vote for a business man for the
place.
