RAILROAD SANDLOTISM.
THE evening organ of the railroad and sandW
combination on Saturday last was in an
of vulgar enjoyment. It clapped its
kicked up its heels, threw sand in every direc
nd literally reveled in its diversified infatua-
Railroad proletarianism could scarcely have
tore vividly illustrated. Its recent tears must
temporarily allayed and it must have been
i that the wave of righteous indignation that
overwhelmed it- corrupt representatives in the Board
( i Supervisors and in other quarters had subsided.
The favorite character of the railroad impressed
upon its newspaper apencies is that of a bully. When
the people n>>e in indignation at the obtru-i<>n of
Han Hums as Mr. Huntingdon's candidate for the
Federal Senatorship. they were promptly slapped in
c. The same course was pursued when the
Market Street Railway <. ompany directed its munici
pal subordinates to donate t-> it in one bunch what
ever easements and privileges San Francisco still re
tained, ror a Â»hort time, however, the emblematical
warning of hemp stilled this sandlot clamor in the
chamber of the Supervisors and a violent demand
mporarily reduced to a comparatively modest
solicitation.
' 'â  Saturday last, however, the original instinct for
I dictation, developed by incessant practice for
a generation, reasserted its controlling power over
the sandloted monopoly. It roared and lied and
shook it- fists and beat the air until it produced one
of the old-fashioned "tempests in a teapot." It did
1 omparatively decent lying about Judge Bur
nett s decision that the Yrooman act is unconstitu
tional and only perverted that ruling into an
imaginary objection to the new charter. But, on the
"corkscrew" franchise, it outdid itself. This com
munity had supposed tbat the attempted resuscita
abandoned franchises on Geary street, between
Taylor and Grant avenue, through an application for
il privileges was designed to forestall the ter
mination of the cable franchise under which the
Geary-street railway is now operated p.nd to deprive
the public of a possible boulevard. It had also been
proclaimed that this part of the associated villainies
had been abandoned. But the evening mouthpiece
of the railway emitted a blast upon this subject that
was enough to deafen all the jackasses in town. It
not only declared tbat the '"corkscrew" should pull
the municipal cork out of Geary street, amidst the
clamations of "property-owners, merchants and
citizens," thus reduced to unassimilating groups, but
.- .signed as a reason that from Taylor street to
Grant avenue is four blocks and that, under the law,
a competing railway could occupy the tracks for five.
The particular competing railway referred to is the
Geary-street cable line, of which the Market Street
Railway Company owns 60 per cent of thfe capital
ck, as it also owns the tracks on Market street
for seven blocks from the intersection of Kcarny
â  to the city front.
tt is apparent that the crushed spirit of the mon
opoly is reviving and that it actually deludes itself
with the belief that the public suppose the Market
Street Railway Company and tl^e Geary Street Rail
way Company to be competitors, that an auction sale
cf the new franchises within the twists of the "cork
-1 rew" would attract more than one bidder, and that
the protest of the Merchants' Association is a forgery.
In official circles in Hawaii President McKinley is
undoubtedly regarded as the best man that ever held
the position. lie has given orders there shall be
no elections in the islands this fall, and all office
holders shall continue to hold and to draw salaries
until further notice.
Kinbassador Choate's latest public appearance was
at a "charity dinner" in London, and in his speech he
s.iid that on his return home he would introduce
< harity dinners here, so we have now one strong rea
son for hoping he will hold his job until he dies. â¢
With Dreyfus going home from Devil Island and
Paty dv Clam in prison, it is clear those who predicted
the affair would result in a revolution have some rea
son to boast their prophecies have been fulfilled.
The Dewey monument fund subscriptions now ex
ceed $30,000, and the question of the hour is, Have
you made your contribution yet?
John D. Rockefeller could not have been so much
pleased with San Francisco as he pretended, for we
notice he didn't buy it
ARE EXPRESS COMPANIES ABOVE
THE LAW?
THE CALL early and repeatedly has referred
to the illegal and unpatriotic attempt, of Wells,
Fargo & Co. to compel its patrons to bear the
cost of the internal revenue stamps, temporarily
exacted by the Federal Government on packages
transmitted by express, under an act of Congress
passed to raise extraordinary revenue during the war
with Spain.
The propriety and the validity of the law were be
yond dispute. Nor has there ever been any serious
question that the infinitesimal tax was due from com
mon carriers, engaged in the business of transporting
packages, and not from their customers. The lan
guage of the act is plain and any other construction
of its terms would be absurd. As we have frequently
observed also such judicial decisions as have been
actually rendered have .almost uniformly sustained the
views we have expressed.
The united action of the press has produced some
effect even upon so hardened, tho\tgh so recent, a
sinner as Wells, Fargo & Co. Press bundles are
forwarded without the imposition of the burthen of
the internal revenue charge. In other directions also
the express corporation has yielded to the sugges
tions of policy. But it has only ''stooped to con
quer." Practically and essentially it defies the law
and the government in the most inexcusable forms in
which such defiance can be expressed or acted.
Inspired and governed by the identical policy that
Mr. Huntington invariably applies in his railroad
management, in respect t<"> litigation pending as we
understand the fact both in State and Federal courts,
it has adopted the Fabian tactics of delay. An au
thoritative and final decision could have been easily
and promptly obtained if Well?, Fargo & Co. had
acted in good faith and upon business rules which
merchants and other ordinary citizens recognize.
Rut it has successfully managed indefinitely to post
pone a result that it knows to be inevitable, and,
while we do not suppose that the courts aided the
plan of procrastination, certainly their acquiescence
has facilitated a practice of pilfering that is equally
condemned by statutory law and by the moral code.
While Congress and the executive department of
the Government, so to speak, are "held up" by Wells,
Fargo & Co., it continues, with a multitude of dis
criminations that in themselves are a wholesale in
fraction of the rule of absolute equality applicable
to common carriers, to transfer every possible cent
of internal revenue stamp expenditure from itself to
the public. It exceeds even this measure of iniquity,
for it favors or punishes individuals according to
their independence or their submissiveness. A study
of the grain rate schedule lately ratified by the obse
quious Railway Commission, where large reductions
are made in places from which no grain is shipped
and the centers of grain production, away from rail
road competition, plundered, will convey a fair idea
of the tyrannous contempt for law that Wells, Fargo
& Co. shares with Mr. Huntington.
The most perfect illustration of equality before the
law that can be derived from textbooks and from au
thorities iÂ« its application to common carriers, which
deal with large numbers of people of every class, oc
cupation and business. Discrimination by common
carriers, especially when exercised arbitrarily and
from motives of mere cupidity or revenge, is insup
portable under a free and stable government. Yet
this is the rule that for thirty years Mr. Huntington
has imposed upon this State and which Wells, Fargo
& Co. is now tenaciously enforcing. The relations
between this corporation and the people for forty
years or ninrc were substantially harmonious. It is
unfortunate that they are now so rudely and so un
necessarily disturbed.
We insist that the courts move with reasonable
celerity in the revenue stamp litigation and end the
delay which covers persistent robbery. All citizens,
moreover, who respect themselves and the laws to the
making of which they have contributed, should stand
upon their rights and refuse to yield to illegal and
unconscionable exactions.
A good beginning was made yesterday in the Cir
cuit Court of the United States when, upon the ap
plication of Attorney General Ford, Judge Morrow
sent back to the Superior Court the petition for a
mandamus filed by Attorney General Fitzgerald to
compel Wells, Fargo & Co. to stamp and transmit
a package deposited in .order to make a test case.
This is a substantial victory that should be vigorously
followed up.
THE VROOMAN ACT
IN The Call of April 9, 1899, the decision of the
Supreme Court of the United States in the case of
the Village of Norwood vs. Baker was referred to
as being of far-reaching importance in its application
to street improvements. At that time we pointed out
the fact that our own Supreme Court had merci
fully held that on that kind of work nothing more
than the land itself could be confiscated, and that,
where the value of a lot was insufficient to pay the
assessment, there could be no judgment against the
owner for the deficiency, but that in the Village of
Norwood vs. Baker, upon grounds arising under the
Federal constitution, the intelligible rule had been
established that no recovery against property-owners
for a street improvement could be had unless the re
sulting benefits substantially covered the assessment,
or under a statute which did not provide for an in
quiry into the fact.
Since that article was written, in the case of Peta
luma Paving Company vs. Singley et al., Judge Bur
nett, one of the Superior Judges of Sonoma County,
upon the authority of the decision above mentioned,
has declared the Vrooman act of 1885, as subsequently
amended, to be unconstitutional, on the ground that
it authorizes the expense of street improvements to be
levied upon adjoining lots by the front foot and with
out reference to benefits. The judgment will have to
stand the test of the Supreme Court of this State, but
the full opinion of Judge Burnett has been published
and it very strongly supports his conclusion. The
Vrooman act has been considered by our Supreme
Court in numerous rases and upheld against at
tacks upon its constitutionality arising under particu
lar sections of the State constitution, and for a num
ber of years it has been the established law of this
State. But in the case of Davies vs. the City of Los
Angeles (86 California Reports, pp. 57-58), where the
statute of 1889, providing specially for the opening,
widening and closing of streets, which for these pur
poses was modeled upon the Vrooman act, was un
der fire, Chief Justice Beatty, in his concurring
opinion, declared that statute unconstitutional be
cause "it permits assessments upon the property sup
posed to be benefited in excess of the benefits." The
learned Chief Justice thus anticipated the decision
upon which Judge Burnett has founded his important
ruling..
The principle upon which adjoining property
owners have been forced to pay for improvements
upon unaccepted streets and, within specified districts,
the cost of opening or widening streets, is that the
work enhanced the value of their lots to a degree that
substantially corresponded with or surpassed the
{ amount of the assessments. It is manifest that this
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, JUNE 13, 1899.
principle is obliterated and arbitrary confiscation,
substituted when the question of benefits is ignored.
It is now settled that State legislation that produces
this result is directly in violation of article XIV, sec
tion i, of the amendments to the Federal constitution,
which inhibits the States from depriving any person
of his property without due process of law.
Many decisions throughout the United States toler
ating the appropriation of city lots for the benefit of
speculators and contractors, and some of them, after
a lien has been exhausted, going so far as to justify
personal judgments for deficiencies against property
owners, have been wiped out of existence by the
just and salutary ruling of the Supreme Court of the
United States. The fate of Judge Burnett's decision
will be awaited with great interest. It is sincerely to
be hoped that several recent decisions upon which
The Call has commented, and which are clearly for
the public welfare, and rest upon propositions that
are as intelligible to the ordinary citizen as to the
bar and to the bench, may be allowed to exercise their
full weight and influence and that they may not be
emasculated through the subtlety or the ingenuity of
corporations or street improvement rings.-
ANOTHER IMPERIALIST CRITIC.
r\ NOTHER correspondent raises an issue about
/ \ the occupancy of the Philippines. He appears
to believe that we began the war on Spain, not
to right the wrongs of the people whom she op- '
pressed, and whose aspirations for independence and
self-government she had denied and defeated, but for
the purpose of conquest and that "forcible annexa
tion,"' which President McKinley denounces as
''criminal aggression."
We need not repeat that this policy was expressly
disavowed by Congress and the President, in the
declaration of war. Nothing has occurred since to
make necessary the repudiation of our solemn pledge
against conquest.
Our latest critic uses the island of Panay and Span
ish occupancy of Iloilo to develop the imperialist
principle of conquest and the spoils of war, as an ex
cuse for practicing "criminal aggression," where we
had promised the Cubans, Porto Ricans and Fili
â¢pinos, and pledged the world, that we would appear
only as a good Samaritan.
Now as to the facts about Luzon and Panay.
Luzon was entirely in the hands of an organized na
tive government, except Manila 'and a narrow zone
around the bay, when Dewey went there. The na
tives closely invested Manila and Spanish sovereignty
extended no farther from the water's edge and the
walls of the city than a rifle shot. It is doubtful if
Montij o had in all the Spanish fleet a gunner who
could have hit the island, unless by accident. If the
doctrine of the spoils of war is to prevail, the Filipinos
have the best title to Luzon, because they were born
there, they own the land and by force had ended
Spanish jurisdiction over most of it without any help
from us. There was the same situation in Panay.
The Spanish held only the capital. Iloilo, the natives
had taken all the rest away. After we had forced
Spain to treat for peace, the natives continued their
investment of Iloilo and finally forced the Spanish
General Rios, to surrender to them, which he did.
Our flag was not there when the Mag of Spain was
hauled down. We had no military nor naval force
there. The Spanish surrender to the natives made
them, by the laws of war, the sovereigns of Panay.
They made the conquest, forced the surrender and
ended Spanish jurisdiction, themselves, with no help
from us. Panay was theirs before our flag went there.
As sovereignty passed over to them they proceeded
to exercise it. Many Europeans were in Iloilo, the
' owners of property and managers of business enter
prises. The native government was a civilized gov
ernment, able to protect and enforce the rights of per
son and property. It performed its public, govern
mental functions so well that business proceeded and
: life went on, with good order and no single case of
: violated rights. Not a dollar's worth of property was
destroyed nor was any one restrained of freedom.
We know of no way in which to prove that Spain
had sovereignty to sell in Panay. If she did, then I
England has some for sale in the United States, and
can dispose of it to a syndicate of nations, which may
throw their navies upon our coasts and begin' bom
bardment of our cities and blockade of our harbors,
without any declaration of war or demand upon us to
surrender. They can appear simply as the owners of
what they have paid for, just as the United States
appeared at Iloilo, and can do as they please with us,
provided we are unable to resist them.
All of Panay remained under the undisputed sover
eignty of the native government until the army and
navy of the United States appeared before Iloilo with
the Spanish deed to the island. General Miller was
in command of the whole expedition, but his subordi
nate, the naval officer in command of the ships, with
out notice to Miller, sent a flag ashore to notify the
foreign Consuls and residents tljat if the city did not
surrender he would wait twenty-four hours and then
shell it. He hardly waited for the return of his mes
senger, but began shelling the town within two hours
of his notice. At the moment his bombardment
began General Miller, his superior and commander
in-chief, was in his cabin on the Newport arranging
with three commissioners representing the native
government of Panay, for our peaceful occupation of
the island. The beginning of the shelling ended the
conference and the commissioners were detained on
board. Much has been said about the Filipinos vio
lating the laws of war and disregarding a flag of
truce, but we doubt whether they have ever been
guilty of anything as flagrant as the shelling and de
struction of Panay. Foreign residents lost millions
oi dollars in property and in due time our taxpayers
at home will have to pay the losses, when the claims
I are proved and put in the hands of their respective
governments.
It appears then that on May 9, 1898, Iloilo was in
possession of Spain. It was garrisoned by that sov
ereign power. It was not in the hands of the natives
at that date. But it finally surrendered to them and
not to us. If there are to be no differences on facts,
we hope pur critic will notice these facts and reconcile
our shelling of Iloilo with our declaration of war
against Spain, or with any right of conquest we may
I hold as against her, or with any title acquired by pur
chase of her sovereignty in Panay, which she had
parted with by surrender to. the natives before the
treaty of Paris had been ratified by either Govern
ment, and, therefore, did not have what she pretended
to sell and we pretended to buy. We can buy exactly
] the same title to Venice from Austria, or to Alasace-
Lorraine from France, or to the Netherlands from
j Spain, that she sold us to Panay and to ninety-nine
I hundredths of the area of the Philippine archipelago.
In their efforts to hit upon an acceptable arbitra
tion scheme the delegates to the Czar's Peace Con
ference made one fatal mistake. They submitted too
many drafts. That would explain the numerous
cases of cold feet.
William F. Herrin has again shuffled the cards and
the Railroad Commission has dealt the farmers of the
State a new grain rate schedule. Who will find the
'joker" in the pack'

TO BEGIN AN
INVESTIGATION
A Complaint Against
Contractors.
WORK ON HALL OF JUSTICE

parent from the delays that have and are

tensions of time were granted on one pre
text or another until July 1. 1599, to finish
and complete said contract; and
Whereas, The owners of the contiguous
property and in that portion of the city

and inconvenience by reason of said delay;
therefore,

open and Improved at the expense of the.
city nnd county, and not to Impose a further
burden upon the property owners; that be
fore a modern pavement shall be laid
the property owners fronting on any ac
cepted block or crossing shall pay one-half
of the expense, which is c/intrary to the
letter and spirit of the law, and the said
cobble and other pavements on the streets
are allowed to remain, to the utter discom
fort, loss and damage to property owners,
whereas, in the interest of the taxpayers,
the funds which could be so use,l are de
voted to other schemes wholly unauthorized
by law. and in this way, with official sanc
tion, they absorb the money which could
be applied to this purpose, to which his
Honor the Mayor's attention Is also
called.
The resolution was referred to the City
and County Attorney for his opinion as to
what steps should bo taken in the matter.

ANOTHER CALL CRITIC
To the Editor of The Call: I read with
much interest the very able letter in your
issue of the 6th inst. about the "Call and
Expansion" and your reply thereto. You
say that "all of the Philippines, except a
few localities on Manila Bay, were occu
pied by the natives, who had wrested
them from Spain before our flap went
there," etc. Under date of May 9, IS9B.
you say under the heading- of "There Is
More Work for Dewey" the followirg:
"Acting Admiral Dewey. the conqueror of
Manila, still has considerable work to per
form before the destruction of Spanish
power in the Far East is complete. Iloilo,
the capital of the Island of Panay. which
is fortified and garrisoned by Spanish
troops, remains to be taken, and there are
one or two point? in the Philippines which
the guns of his fleet will be called on to
reduce."
Now. our flag had been there nine days,
and before it went there, say on the 30th
of April. IS9S. Iloilo was not in the hands
of the natives, and it la not a locality of
Manila Bay. Seeing there can be no dif
ferences on facts, will you kindly publish
and explain? Yours respectfully,
WILLIAM STILL.

J. A. Johnson of the Thirteenth Minne

I The repairs to the transport Sheridan's

Captain George Ankers was not drowned
from Long Wharf. Oakland. For several
months his wife and family have mourned
him for dead, but three days ago a well
known shipmaster met him in Portland
and had a long conversation with him.
Ankers said he was not coming back' to
San Francisco until he had earned enough
money to make good the amount he was
short in the accounts of the schooner
Oceania Vance. In th" meantime his
wife Is making a brave struggle in Mill
Valley to support her children.
Captain Ankers was formerly master of
the Ala (I company's steamer San Juan.
hut during one voyage she went aground
and according to a rule of the company
Ankers lost his command. He Was then
made master of The schooner Oceania
Vance and made several trips to Hawaii
in hr-r. On his last voyage the vessel
went to Oakland to discharge. Captain
Ankers had been to the office of the own
ers on his arrival and the next day was
to settle Up his accounts. That night he
â¢ rossed over on the ferry and had dinner
on the steamer with some friends. He had
not been heard from until a friend of his
ran across him in Portland.
The general supposition was that Ank
ers had fallen overboard and been
drowned. A reward of $50 was offered for
his remains and for weeks men dragged
in the vicinity of the wharf in hopes of
earning the money. His wife hoped
against hope that he would be heard
from, but finally came to the conclusion
that he was dead and set herself to the
task of providing for her family. The
amount Ankers was short in his accounts
was not very great and the chances are
that had he made a clean breast of it to
his owners they would have given him a
chance to retrieve himself. He told his
friend in Portland that he would secure a
position as mate on some vessel and try
to work his way back into his old posi
tion.
I The Italian bark Stefano Razeto was on
fire in Sydney harbor yesterday, but was
not greatly damaged. She left here on
March 9 with 758.406 feet of lumber and
some general cargo, the whole being val
ued at $98,159. She arrived at Sydney, N.
S. \V., on May 31; and the supposition is
that the fire broke out in the general
cargo.
The steam schooner Celia Is on her way
back to San Francisco after an absence
of three years. She was formerly engaged
in the coasting and lumber trade, but was
chartered to some mine owners for use
on the Mexican coast. She is coming herÂ»
I for an overhauling and Is bringing up a
cargo of cedar logs.
The steamer Noyo took away for Fort
Bragg yesterday a party of campers that
expects to do great things in the way of
hunting and fishing. They are Eric and
Alfred Rosenstirn, Clifford Cook and W.
Scoofy. With them went three horses, a
wagon, half a dozen hunting dogs and
about a ton of supplies. They are bound
for Eel River and during- their six weeks*
stay expect to do some wonderful things
in the -way of hunting and fishing.

THE HYDERABAD NEAR TO DESTRUCTION.
A WEEK ago Mondny the big ship Hyderabad wrs caught in a terrific storm. A heavy sea struck her
under the counter and disabled her steering gear. The ship became unmanageable Rnd went over
until her lee rail was under water. Sails hud to be cut loose in order to save the masts, and when
that danger was past Captain Scott found his vessel almost on the Farallones. The decks were full
of water and five men were injured wiiile trying to brace the yards. Finally to save his vessel from the
rocks the three lower topsails had to be cut away and then the Hyderabad escaped by a shave.
were several consignments for Manila â 
and all the passengers she had accommo
dation for.

ON TEMPERANCE
Eva Marshall Shoutz.
MISS EVA MARSHALL
SHOUTZ, who has been
styled the new Jeanne
d'Arc, will lecture on tem
perance at the First T'nited Pres
byterian Church next Thursday
evening.
Eva Shoutz has always been a
devoted temperance advocate and
worker. Her strong temperance
principles were in a measure In
herited, since both her mother and
her father have been and ar<
est temperance per, pie, and her
steadfastness, intensity and gift
for sacrifice m ty also have com- to
her through heredity, as she is
descended from Huguenots, who
suffered persecution and prison for
their faith, so that she may al
most be said to have the blood of
saints and martyrs in her veins.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, JUNE 13. 1899.
MAYOR PHELAN`S
CAMPAIGN FOR
MOTHER TERM
Activ.iy on the Line of
Supporters.
THOSE NEW SUPERVISORS
â¢
SUGGESTION THAT THE MAYOR
SHALL NAME NOMINEES.
Once He Named a Board So Bad
That He Had to Demand Its
Expulsion From
Office.
It is generally accepted as a fact that
the committee of seven appointed by
the General Committee of One Hun
dred Democrats will report a scheme of
apportionment that will give the small
est subdivisions of Assembly districts
represented in the nominating conven
tion. There is no longer talk of a blan
ket ticket and the Lawrence scheme for
reducing the General Committee to
twenty-five has gone glimmering.
While Buckley is doing some quiet
but effective work in Hub organization
throughout the city he is not putting
a straw in the way of Mayor Phelan's
renomination. The friends at Phelar.
assert that the Mayor's renomination is
already assured. He is getting many
Democratic club indorsements and it is
plain to the observer that the resolu
tions of indorsement emanate from a
central point. There is an occasional
variation. For instancy, over on Stock
ton street the pure-minded Demc-crats
in one long respiration indorse Mayor.
Phelan and denounce the Twinkling
Star Improvement Company and the
Hotel Nymphia.
Phelan's friends are so confident of
his success that they are demanding
that he should be permitted to name
his own Board of Supervisors. If this
demand is urged there will be trouble
in the Democratic party. It is called to
mind by Democrats that the preroga
tive of naming the Supervisors was
once conferred upon Mr. Phelan, and
that such a horrible bad job was made
by him in the selection that he had to
join Deacon Fitch and the courts in
efforts to expel the selected from
<>fii< I-. Phelan may know a good Mayor
when he sees one in shadow or sub
stance, but there is something wrong
with his sight and scent for Supervi
sor*. It is on record that ho had a fair
trial once, with a field of good material
before him. and after calm reflection
picked out as bad a crowd as ever dis
graced the city.
Many wise Democrats are in favor
of delegating to the nominating con
vention the duty of selecting candidates
for the Board of Supervisors, but the
shouting Democrats having the upper
hand at present want Phelan placed
in supreme control. Eighteen Supervi
sors of Phelan'p selection supplemented
with Esola as Chief of Police would
give the new charter such a test as the
framers of that organic act never ap
prehend cd . _____________

RADGER BADLY BEATEN.
J. Blals, a barber at 36 Sixth street,
and L. Heidlngcr. his assistant, appeared
before Judge Graham yesterday to an
swer to a charge of battery. The com
plaining witness was J. Badger, a barber
at 26V4 Sixth street, who until a few
months ago worked for Blais.
From the evidence it appeared that
Heidinger told Blals that Badger was
bragging about having taken Lena,
Blais beautiful daughter, who is not yet
OUt of her teens, to a saloon. June 1 Blaia
sent word to Badger that he wanted to
Be* him in his shop and Badger crossed
over. He was taken to a rear room and
Blais asked him if he had said that he
had taken Lena to a saloon, and he de
nied it. He said he had invited her to
dine with him. but she did not keep the
appointment. This did not satisfy Blais.
and with Heldlnger's assistance he gave
Badger a terrific beating, blackening his
eyes.
Lena was in court and denied that she
was ever in a saloon with Badger, but
admitted that he had asked her to dine
with him. The Judge convicted the two
defendants and ordered them to appear
for sentence this morning.

OFFICIALS OF
HEALTH BOARD
WILL DIE HARD
Bring Su t to Enjoin
Their Dismissal. .
FIFTEEN ARE PLAINTIFFS
CLAIM THEY ARE STATUTORY
OFFICERS.
If Their Contention Is Upheld They
Will Continue to Hold Office
for Several Months to
Come.
It se*ms that the large majority of pub
lic officials appointed during the last ad
ministration have found their positions
quite desirable, and in conseouenre when
dismissal confronts them they have deter
mined to dl*> hard. Stilt was ftled yester
day by fifteen under officials In the Health
Office to enjoin tho new hoard from dis
pensing with their services and appointing
others to fill the vacancies.
The plaintiffs In the action are: Otto
Lunders. health inspector; Thomas II
McOrath, health inspector; P. F. Ward,
plumbing inspector; J. J. Dunne, secre
tary; J. (TKane. superintendent of city
cemeteries; Benjamin Davis, chief market
inspector; William Finnigan, health in
spector; James \V. Crowe health inspec
tor: J. W. ORourke, veterinary surgeon;
James F. Lahane'y, food Inspector; J. J.
Sullivan, chief plumbing inspector; J. L.
David, health inspector; T. Donovan,
health Inspector; A. R. Happersberger.
superintendent of dislnterrrtents and in
spector of vaults, and W. H. Harrison,
Inspector of garbage and transportation.
The new Board of Health, through fin al
leged threat to dispose of all the plain
tiffs named are made defendants in the
action. On' the filing of the complaint
Judge Dalngerfieki issuer] an order to
show cause, directing the defendants to
appear on the 23d inst. and show cause
why they should not be restrained from
dismissing the plaintiffs named.
Plaintiffs hold that they are statutory
officers and hold for four years after their
appointment. A majority of the plain
tiffs were appointed in the latter months
of the year ISPS, and in consequence if the
court holds they are statutory officers
have several months yet to serve. Dunne
& McPlke represent the anxious officials
and will wagf their right in the courts
against any attempt to remove them be
fore the time fixed by law has expired.
FOR A FULL TERM.
County Officials Bring Their Action
, Against the Charter.
Action on behalf of Sheriff Martin,
County Clerk I 'Pane and Recorder Ber
thler against the Election Commissioners
to enjoin the defendants from placing the
offices held by the plaintiffs on the elec
tion proclamation and ballots was file.l
yesterday by Attorney George D. Col
lins. The complaint was' filed after the
commission had passed a resolution re
-1 fusing to comply with the demand of tUe
. officials and strike off the offices named.
In the complaint It is first set forth
that the plaintiffs were regularly elected
to the offices which they now hold and
were elected for a stated term of years,
; and hence their removal before such pe
riod has expired would be in direct con
j flict with the constitution, an invasion of
; and an infringement upon the sovereignty
i of the State, a revolutionary usurpation
1 of power and In violation of the law en-
I titled "An act to establish a uniform
system of county and township govern
ments." approved April 1. 1K97.
It Is said in the complaint that the ac
tion is not designed to Impeach the valid
ity of the charter but to protect and gain
for the plaintiff officials their rights un
der the law. In conclusion an injunction
is asked for, as stated.
CONTRIBUTION BOX BOBBED.
Burglars Enter St Paul's Catholic
Church.
The contribution box of St. Paul's
Catholic Church on Twenty-ninth and
Church streets was robbed yesterday
morning at an early hour. The box had
bren placed in the old church to receive
donations to the new edifice being erected,
and it is supposed that the thieves se
cured about $60. The money was in en
velopes superscribed with the names of
the donors, and the finding of a number
of these envelopes in a vacant lot near
by led to the discovery of the theft. De
tective Dillon and Fitzgerald were nut
on the* case, but have been unable to nnd
a clew to the thieves.
John W. Taylor's Lecture.
John W. Taylor, ex-Superintendent of
Schools, will give an illustrated lecture on
"The Philippines" at Sherman & (.'lay's
Music Hall this evening. He will speak
of affairs at Manila and adiacent coun
try with knowledge derived from personal
observation. He was there when the ac
tive fighting began and went on the firing
line with the troops. He studied the
country, its climate and its productive
capacity. In the course of his travels he
obtained three hundred photographs. The
lecture will entertain and enlighten the
audience.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
Unusual conditions are shewn upon the j
â¢weather map. A depression of great depth
overlies Arizona and Southeastern California. ;
At Independence, Yuma and Phoenix the '
barometer reading is ITelow 29.50 Inches. The
pressure it- relatively high over Oregon and
Washington. Both â¢'- barometric and thermo- :
metric gradients are unusually steep along the 1
coast of California,
The temperature ha* fallen generally over
the country west of the Rocky Mountains'.
Over Utah and Nevada it has fallen 16 de
crees.
Rain has fallen in small quantities at Los
Angeles and at San r>tÂ°BO.
The following maximum wind velocities are
reported: Eureka, 26 miles per hour from the
northwest; Winnemucca, 26 west; El Paso. 36
west: Idaho Falls. 36 south; Mount Tamalpals,
TS west.

hours ending midnight, June 13, ISP9:

EASTERN MARKETS.
NKW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. June 12.â There was evidence
of large buying: by powerful financial interests
In a limited number of stocks anil of manipula
tion to advance : licet in the stock market to
day. Speculation otherwise was in a some
what quiescent state. Outside of the special
capes where prices were support ed the busi
ness done was rather heavy. Many of the rail
roads ranged downward within the limits of a
point during the early trading. There was an
organized buying throughout the- day of Fed
eral Steel, which was marked up nearly 3 [
points and closed almost at the top. There was
some sentimental effect by this on other steel
ftorks. American Steel and Wire responding
late in Uie day on covering by shorts, but
none of the gains in the group were well held
outside of Federal Steel. The so-called Flower
stocks were all well supported and Brooklyn
Transit shared Its strength with Metropolitan
Street Railway and Manhattan. This group of
New York traction stocks showed the effects
of realizing from time, to time during the day
a â '. Mnnho-Kon wo. djn aa nj below la.-t night's
close by its weight. The grangers were taken
up at one time, St. Paul in the lead, but did
not retain ail of the gain and In some In-
Ms-nces more than lost it. The coalers were
pretty well sustained also. Other points of .
ft- gth were Chicago Terminal preferred.
Denver and Rio Grande Issues and the newly
itsued Wisconsin Central stocks. Sugar fluc
tuated up and down during the whole day. but I
always above Saturday's level. It rose at one i
time 3 points, but lost it all before the cl se.
There was no evidence of outside demand
to koep the market up and the railroad group
showed Itself affected by. the heavy tone of the
foreign Stock Exchanges, on account of political
disquietude. The announcement of the resig
nation of ' the French Ministry helped the late
Traction in prices. The engagement of an ad
ditional S2,fiOO.Ono of gold for export to-morrow,
although the probability of it had been Inti
mated, also weighed on the market. No effect
was apparent in the money market, which con
tinued decidedly easy. Exchange also, though '
It hardened a shade, continues a broad fraction
below the point where experts can figure a
profit on gold exports. It Is, in fact, generally
admitted that the gold movement is due to thÂ»
actual buying of gold In this market to re
plenish the reserve of the Hank of England and
probably also Cor Russian purposes. This is a
precisely similar operation to that undertaken
by the United States Government through a
syndicate to replenish the gold in the Treasury
during the last administration. It is to be
noted that while the foreign money markets
are thus drawing on New York for gold as the
mo.n available source New York banks are
offering special inducements, such as paying
the exprcssage on bills remitted by Interior
correspondents, to secure a supply of legal
tender notes to meet the demand of active
business
The market for bonds was dull and sluggish
and changes are mixed. Total sales, par value
J2.5M.000. United States 4's and the s's de
clined Sk in the bid mice.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
j CHICAGO, June 12.â The effect of the un-
I expectedly bullish Government crop report
given out Saturday was apparent at the open
ing in wheat, but It was Boon shown that
| other influences had superseded it in the minds
lof speculators. The opening in July ranged
I all the way from 76 5 i77c, compared with
, Saturday's closing price of 75 l /4C. It sold at
I once at 76>4c, but rallied almost immediately
to 76!feC. But in this little spasm of nervous
, ness was dissipated all the effect of the Gov
i ernment report. The selling was heavy on the
bulge. Commission houses were ' in the mar
ket with big lots. For a short time the mar
ket held up under the liquidation, but finally
; broke, 76% c being touched nerore any reaction
; occurred. Heavy flour sales reported from
the Northwest caused a cessation of liquidation
i from that direction, but In the meantime local
holders had apparently lost confidence and the
offerings at all times exceeded the demand.
The enormous Western movement became the
, one factor as the session advancd. The Mani
toba Government report gave an Increased
wheat acreage with an almost perfect con
i dition. Domestic ' crop news was unfavorable
- as regards winter wheat, but favorable as to
Fprlng wheat. . The market became very weak
toward the close on the announcement of the
! estimated receipts of 400 cars. July finally
i sold down to 7o'/ic and closed at 75'4@75%c.
â¢ Corn early was steady with wheat. Fears
that the strike in Buffalo would again mi
i volve the elevators weakened the market later
and prices gradually slid off.
Oats were independently strong. There was
I again a good demand, mainly for the deferred
I futures, some of the foreign account, and the
most bullish crop report restored prices dur
. ing the decline of ether grains. Selling was
scattered. Receipts were 167 cars. July closed
! Vie higher.
Provisions were steady considering the large
run of hogs and lower yard prices. There
was moderate general buying demand and sell
; Ing was scattered. Trade on the whole was
I small and prices kept within a narrow range
t At the close July pork was unchanged, lard
2 1 , c higher and ribs 2' 4 c higher.

At a point 94 feet south from the west cross
cut that passes the winze station they have
cut out for two sill sets, and while doing so
have extracted 22 cars, equal to about SO tons
of ore of the avers Be assny value of, gold,
HI "4, nnd sliver. 3.41 ounces per ton. Part of
this ore is from old timbers and part from new
ground. They have no regular force at work
in this ground an yet. hut men will be put
to work on this ore as soon as repairs are
completed on the main hoist. IKRO levelâ The
station on this level has been used for shaft
work. 1950 levelâ This level wa* recovered on
the 7th Inst., and after the station had been
examined they started to clean and repair It.
; The station is one consisting of a regular
: working and pump-bob station, and it will
I have to be thoroughly timbered and rearranged.
â  From the station the main west drift has heen
closed by timbers that they have not yet dis
turbed, as below the station there Is a large
stream of water flowing into the shaft that may
he running from a bulkhead on the level. Be
fore opening this level the station will be
thoroughly impaired. They have secured all the
defective timbers and the station is safe for
the present. On the surface the main hoist Is
being repaired and will probably be finished in
about five days. While this work is being
done all underground work will be suspended

TO KEEP FOOD CRISP.
A novel way of opening a package of
food is shown on the Grape-Nuts pack-
age, where a line indicates that a slit
should be made with a knife, and the
pack;. squeezed, which makes it gap
enough to pour out what is needed for
a meal, then the package automatically
closes, preserving the contents from the
moisture of the air.
As a rule Grape-Nuts packages are
not kept on hand very long in any
family, but it is well to know how to
keep the contents of the package in
prime condition. Grape-Nuts are ready
cooked, very crisp, and can be served
immediately with a little good cream or
milk. This feature is of great advant-
age to those who appreciate ease and
convenience in preparing breakfast.

A Far Reaching Decision.
OAKLAND, June 12.â 1n the suit of the
Piedmont Paving Company vs. John All
man, for the collection of unpaid assess
ments for street improvements. Judge
Greene to-day decided that under the
Vrooman act th>- clerk of a City Council
ther legislative body has no power to
fix the time up to which or on which the
ell will receive or consider bids for
street work. The decision is far reaching:,
for if upheld by the Supremo Court it will
invalidate every recent assessment for
street Improvements in Oakland.
Nance O'Neil True to Her Father.
OAKLAND. June 12.â George K. Lamson
has given notice that he will shortly move
to have the order directing him to pay
his divorced wife, Annie F. Lamson, $30
pc r month, revoked. He is the father of
Nance O'Neil, the actress, and alleges
that his income is decreasing with his ad
vancing years. He is now 72 years old
and avers that but for the assistance ex
-3 to him by his daughter he could
not have paid the alimony thus far.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, JUNE 13. 1899.
POND OF YOLD
MAY BE LABOR
COMMISSIONER
Gage Has No Use for
Trades Unions.
HE PREFERS A POLITICIAN
OAKLAND'S CANDIDATE HAD A
HALF PROMISE.
A Man Who Always Delivered the
Goods Said to Be Pre
ferred Before a Work-
ingman.
Oakland Office Ran Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, June 12.
A quiet tip has been given out that the
next Labor Commissioner will be Wallace
Pond of Berkeley, who has for many
years been a Republican leader In Yolo
County.
Governor Oage is said to have arrived
at the conclusion that he cannot, without
fomenting strife, appoint a new Commis
sioner from the rank of the trades unions.
Some time ago the Governor invited the
trades unions of the State to submit to
him the names of five men who would
be acceptable to them, implying thereby
that from theso five he would select one.
Many names were submitted to him. ac
companied hy petitions and indorsements,
and in many instances the Governor says
he has received counter petitions, till the
matter became so mixed that he could '
not see his way clear to do anything with :
the Indorsements of the unions which â 
regulate labor.
With this alleged excuse as a pretext,
the Governor, it is said, has decided to
name some <~>ne for the place who has
been Interested in politics. Wallace Pond
for many years was a Republican leader
in Yolo County and could always be de
pended upon to keep up his end in every
campaign. Less than a year ago he
moved to Berkeley, whore he now con
ducts a drug store, but he has had all
his friends working for him, and some of
them have enough assurance to justify
them In naming him as a winner.
This decision of the Governor Is a
great disappointment to Oakland, which
city has been passed by by Governor
Gage with remarkable regularity, with
the exception of the alleged gold brick
now in the possession ot Charles Spear.
This city has been interested in putting
forward the claims of A. J. Read, presi
dent of the State Typographical Union
and trustee of the Church of the Advent.
a brother trustee being James J. Valen
tine. Mr. Read has been indorsed by
many trades unions and has received
some encouragement from those who are
pretty close to the Governor, btit it now
appears thru the semi-promises partook
ot the nature of Charley Spear's appoint
ment.
EXPECT A LIVELY SESSION
Teachers Dread the Coining lu.eeting
of Alameda's School Board.
AI.AMF.nA. June 12. -The Board of Ed
ucation will meet to-morrow night t
teachers and a most exciting session is
looked for. Whether or not changes will
be made is a query that no one can an
swer. The breaking of all combinations
through the difference of opinion origi
nating from the payment of the Hallentine
bill has left the directors at sea. and It is
probable that not until the vote is taken
to-morrow night will the members of the
board themselves know what the results
are to be.
The directors undertook to hold a se
cret caucus and one of those present
failed to keep the secret. This so Incensed
tin other membt-rs that they declare they
will never participate in another star
chamber session. In consequence the
merits of teachers will have to be dis
cussed in open meeting and to-morrow
night some of the AJameda pedagogues
wifl probably be given an opportunity to
h<"ar themselves "picked to pieces."
Teachers who have been threatened with
removal have been at work for a week
pulling wires to save their places.

A GLORIOUS FOURTH.
California Soldiers May Return in
Time to Participate.
The executive committee of the Fourth
of July committee met last evening at the
Grand Hotel. Two prizes for the most
handsomely decorated places of business
in this city were offered; the first will be
$30 and the second $20. The following
recommendations for expenditures wore
reported by the finance committee and
accepted: Appropriation for parade, $37">;
music. $1140: regatta. $600: printing, $100;
invitations, $. r >o; press, $300.
Some discussion arose as to whether the
committee should appropriate money for
thf purpose of decorating streets. The
matter was finally referred back to the
finance committee. The sum of $1500 will
bo expended for fireworks. There will he
several displays, and the committee will
meet representative citizens to-morrow
afternoon to discuss the best locations
for displays. A short time ago a request
that the California Volunteers be per
mitted tn leave Manila on such a date as
to arriv- In San Francisco on or before
July 4 was telegraphed to Washington.
Yesterday the following answer was re
ceived:
H.->n. Julius Knhn, San Francisco: Your wish
that the California volunteers return so as to
reach San Franr-lsro by July 4 has been re
relvr-rl and the substance has been cabled to
General Otta Ht Manila.
H. C. CX)RBIN, Adjutant GenerHl.
It has been proposed to postpone the
Fourth of July celebration should the
boys be scheduled to arrive within a few
days after the Fourth.

HUNTERS START FOR
A NEW SEAL ROOKERY
NEXT ATTACK WILL BE MADE
AT POINT ARENA.
A Government Officer Explains Why
He Protested Against the Thin
ning of the Big Herds.
For all that the Federal authorities at
Washington prohibited the killinp of sea
Hi na on the <"Jovernment reservations on
this coast the State Board of Fish Cnm
rrij-sioners is determined tn keep on with
this work In the interests of the fish In
dustries of the State. While it would have
been more convenient to have carried on
the work of thinning the seal herds off
the entrance of the bay and along the
line of the southern run of salmon, still
there are other places where sea lions
abound In goodly numbers. One of these
places is the bIR rookery a little south of
Point Arena, and to this place Deputy Al
Wilson and W. H. Greene, the experienced
seal hunter, went yesterday. They are
equipped for a lonpr stay and for the de
struction of many of the objectionable sea
animals.
The Point Arena rookery is situated
upon a small island, or large rock, cover
lug several acres, and in past years the
seal herds have made it a breeding
ground. Years ago there was a sealing
station there and large numbers were
taken. The rookery was more frequented
by the brown harking seal than any other,
and thoso are the fellows that the Com
missioners are after, as they have proved
tn be the more destructive of salmon and
fishermen's nets.
The Commissioners have received a lonp
letter from T. S. Palmer, Acting chief Âf
the Biological Survey at Washington,
upon the subject of the protest sent by his
department to the Secre,tary of the Navy
and Lighthouse Board to the killing of i
sea lions on the Government reservations
near this port. Incidentally he stated that
he believed that the estimate of 20, aea
liens within a distance of fifty miles of
this port and that they destroy fr.im 20
to 40 per cent of the salmon entering the !
bay Is rather high, and he says: "It is i
only fair to state that at the 'time our
protest wax made we had no official state
ment of the intention of your board or the j
method of carrying out the work other i
than has appeared : n the press reports." 1
Judging from the tone of Mr. Palmer's i
litter he seems to think that the most of
the damage done to the salmon industry
Is caused by the "harbor" seal and not by
the sea lion, and adds:
"In conclusion permit me to say that
our protest was not inspired from mo
tives of sentiment or from any desire to
oppose the action of your board, but it
seems that if a thorough Investigation of
the food habits of the sea lions had not
been made this was the time to make it,
before undertaking a wholesale slaughter
of the animals."
A BOLD HOLD-UP.
The Tall Man and the Short Man
Again at Work.
T. B. I.eet, employed at thp Telephone
Oyster House at California and Pnlk
streets, reported to the police last night
that he hnd heon held up at the corner of
Sacramento and Octavla streets by two
footpads. He had 80 cents In his vest
pocket, but it was not found by the rob
bers. I^eet describee the men as "One
tall and one short." A revolver was in
the possession of one of them, and when
they failed to find anything on his per
son he was struck on the back of the head
with the weapon.
Captain Wittman immediately detailed
several detectives and police on the case.

WHO SILL
PAY ALL THE
COSTS OF SUIT?
Aftermath of the Grain
Rate Case.
BEFORE THE CIRCUIT COURT
A LEGAL QUESTION ON THE DIS
MISSAL OF THE SUIT.
The Railroad Contends That in Re
scinding the Original Resolu
tion the Commission For
feited All Claim.
The question of costs for the grain rate
case tried In the Circuit Court came up
before Judge Morrow yesterday. The case
Itself has been dismissed, but it left a
legacy of litigation in the question of costs
and who should pay them. The last ac
tion in the matter was taken by the State
In a motion to so modify the decree of
dismissal that the State should have its
costs paid by the railroad, and it was this
motion which was under argument yes
terday.
E. S. Pillsbury represented the railroad
and Attorney General Ford and Judge
Hayne appeared for the State, despite t^e
fact that at the last meeting of the Kail
road Commission it was deride. l to dis
pense with special counsel. Mr. Ford
spoke first.
In his argument he contended that tho
railroad had voluntarily dismissed the
suit and therefore by rule of court it was
responsible for the costs. In courts of
equity, he said, a dismissal of the bill of
complaint is taken to be a judgment in
favor of the defendant, and unless there
are extraordinary reasons why the court
should exercise its discretion in favor of
the complainant the defendants are of
right entitled to their costs.
No such reason existed in the cape at
bar, Mr. Ford contended. The railroad
had never rescinded the 8 per cent re
duction: it had rescinded the pre
liminary resolution but it had never re
scinded the schedule itself; in other
words, when the commission rescinded
the resolution it left the schedule of rat.es
still In force and that schedule had never
been dismissed. The railroad, he claimed,
had voluntarily dismissed the action,
abandoned the field and had left the Com
missioners masters of the situation with
the rate complained of still in full force.
That being true, he argued, the rail
road company had in fact admitted that
Its bill was without foundation and its
motion for a dismissal was in fact a
withdrawal from the contest. That being
the case, he reasoned, the company
should ray to the Railroad Commis.sion*
ers the costs they had incurred in the ac
tion.
In answer Mr. Pillsbury reviewed the
history of the case from the time of the
original resolution to the motion of the
State for costs. In September of 1595, he
said the commission passed a resolution
making a horizontal reduction of 25 per
cent on all rates other than grain rates
and a reduction of S per cent on grain
rates. In the succeeding month the rail
road brought suit for an Injunction re
straining the commission from enforcing
the schedule so established. Tn Novem
ber of the next year the injunction was
granted, and Mr. Pillsbury read from the
decision of Judge McKenna in support of
some of the statements ne made about
the case presented by the State. He had
said that the commission admitted the
reduction of 25 per cent could not be en
forced, and the Commissioners would not
try to enforce it. On that point Judge

McKenna wrote he thought the language
of the resolution was plain, and it hal
presumbably been prepared after due in
quiry Into the matter; hut in view of the
statements of the counsel for the com
mission that the schedule would never be
enforced he would dissolve the injunction
as to the 25 per cent reduction and would
further continue the injunction against
the grain rate reduction until the further
order of thf court.
The case ran along then until April of
1898, when the pleadings were settled and
the taking of testimony propeeded. There
had been 3000 pages taken when the new
board went into office in the beginning
of this year. On April 24 last the new
board rescinded both resolutions and di
rected the Attorney General to move to
dismiss the suits without either party
paying the costs of the ither. Some days
after the Attorney General made the mo
tion asking that the cases be dismissed
and that each party be allowed to pay
its own costs. The motion was heard
May S ami was denied on the ruling of
Judge Morrow that the defendant had no
right to move for a dismissal.
In pursuance of this state of affairs, Mr.
Pillsbury contended, the railroad on May
19 last moved to dismiss the case, append
ing to the motion a copy of the resolu
tion of the Railroad Commission rescind
ing the resolutions reducing the rates and
directing the Attorney General to dismiss
the suit, while each party paid ttfl own
costs. The motion or the company was
granted and the order dismissing the ease
was entered, but there was nothing in it
about costs: and a few days later the
State asked to be allowed its costs.
That brought the case up to the session
yesterday, and Mr. Pillsbury proceeded to
draw his conclusions from it.
He argued that in winning on the orig
inal Injunction proceedings the company
was entitled to its costs for that much
of the suit. When the answer of the
State had been put in the company had
made certain objections to it and they
had all been sustained, the company
therefniv stiil being entitled to its costs
It was stated at the time that there was
no money in the appropriaion for the
c;ise to pay costs, and Mr. Pillsbury said
[ the company paid them temporarily"pend
ing the outcome of the case. Then the
taking of testimony began, and after it
hail proceeded as far as three thousand
pages the State moved to dismiss, each
party to pay Its own costs.
In making this motion. Mr. Pillsbury
contended, the State waived all right to
have its costs paid by the railroad. The
company had won on the original injunc
tion, he said; it had won on the objec
tions to the answer, and then, by rescind
ing the resolution upon- which the whole
case had been based, it left the company
with nothing -to sue for and the court
nothing to try. The original resolution
! having caused all the trouble and expense
1 and that having been rescinded. Mr. Pllls
i bury claimed the commissioners were re-
I sponsible for the costs of the suit the
original resolution had caused. By re
scinding the resolution they admitted it
was not right and just, and that was all
the company was fighting for. It was,
in fact, a victory for the company, and
he claimed that while the railroad was
willing to pay its own costs, it should not
be assessed for the costs of the State.
The case will go on again to-morrow
when Mr. Pillsbury will finish his argu
ment.
KILLED BY A FALL.
C. Dunn, a Painter, Meets Death
While at Work in San Mateo
County.
C. Dunn, a painter who resided at 916
Howard street, fell from the roof of a
shed at Captain Cole's residence in San
Mateo County yesterday afternoon and
was picked up unconscious. He was taken
to St. Mary's Hospital, where he died
without regaining consciousness.
Dunn, who was employed by J. H. Keefe
of 317 Sutter street, went down into San
Mateo County yesterday morning in com
pany with James Casey to paint Captain
Cole's residence at that place. During- the
afternoon he climbed up by means of a
rope to fasten some cleats. The rope
parted and precipitated him to the roof
cf a shed. He struggled for a moment to
gain a hold on the edge of the roof, but
slipped and fell to the ground, a distance
of about twenty feet. His skull, knee
and right arm were fractured by the fall
and ho also sustained internal injuries.
Dunn was 44 years of age and unmar
ried. He was a brother of former School
Director J. J. Dunn.

Alcazar.
Lewis Morrison, assisted by Miss Flor
ence Roberts and the Alcazar Stock Com
pany, presented at the Alcazar last night
"The Master of Ceremonies," which had
such a successful run on Its first produc
tion last summer. The cast was the same
with two or three exceptions and nothing
was lost by the change. The two princi
pals met with a flattering reception and
wero several times called before the cur
tain after the close of the second and
third acts. The support was excellent.
Olympia.
Querlta Vincent scored a hit at the
Olympfa last night. Arrayed In the most
dazzling costumes ever seen on the Olym
pia stage, this talented California girl de
livered her "coon" songs and buck and
wing dances in a way that won many
encores. Herbert and Weston came in for
a goodly share of the audience's enthusi
asm, and the same fortune favored the
La Rose brothers.
The Chutes.
Stanley and Scanlon, musical cemedians,
extracted all sorts of music from all sorts
of Instruments at the Chutes Theater last
evening, the "flying" Banvards tossed
each other from one side of the building
to the other, and Mr. and Mrs. Keesing
changed their songs. The rest of the bill
was good.

PHELAN TRAPPED BY
THE GAS COMPANIES
Mayor and the Minority Accept a
Bogus Compromise.
The Corporations Fix Their Own Rates and the City
Abandons Its Right to a Hearing in Court.
Street Franchises Discussed.
Gas and electric light rates to the
muncipalily and to private consumers
â¢have been reduced by the Board of Su
pervisors. Mayor Phelan Is smiling In
the innocent belief that he has won a
victory, very materially political, and
the pas and electric light corporations
are delighted in the fact that they have
humbugged the Mayor, imposed upon
his faithful minority and cheated the
city out of what might have been a
preat public benefit. After a deal of
noise, the blare of political trumpets
and much official fuss and feathers the
Mayor and the minority have accepted
the terms of the corporations, jeopar
dized, if not destroyed, their own action
'â¢in fixing a rale of ?1 10 a thousand feet
and restored to the majority members
of the Board of Supervisors the unques
tioned right to meet as often as they
please and take whatever action they
â wish under the limitations of the con
solidation act.
, By the authority and with the sanc
tion of Mayor Phelan the Board of Su
pervisors met yesterday afternoon and
adopted the very rate for gas which the
3ocaJ corporations were eager to accept
on March L. The Mayor and the minor
ity members of the board have united
with the majority In a betrayal of the
city in its effort to secure a material
reduction In the rates for gas and elec
tric lighting. The Mayor and his fol
lowers in the board have stultified
themselves and have made a complete
sacrifice of any legal right to maintain
the rate of ?1 10 for gas. They have offi
cially adopted an agreement that all
existing controversies between the city
end county and the San Francisco Gas
and Electric Company and Pacific Gas
Improvement Company are formally
(tied.
This means nothing more or less than
an filcial withdrawal of the ?1 10 rate
and an acceptance of a rate of fl 50.
In settling the controversy the Board
of Supervisors adopted a new rate, thus
abandoning the claim that the board
has the right to fix a rate for a year,
and, having fixed a rate, cannot make
another. The very agreement by which
the bnard, under the guidance of the
Mayor, bowed to the gas and electric
light companies was written by an at
torney of the corporation and was
framed to accomplish just what his em
ployers wanted. And the Mayor still
thinks he won a victory.
The vital part of the agreement is
the settlement of the controversy in
which the gas and electric companies
saw that they inevitably must lose. It
is then provided in the resolution which
was adopted yesterday that from July
I gas rates for private consumers shall
"be $1 50 a thousand feet instead of $1 75.
Bates for the city shall be $1 35 a thou
sand feel instead of $1 50 and 10 "cents
a. night for each street lamp instead of
II cents. On electric light bills there
ehall be a reduction of 15 per cent. On
paper and in the confiding brain of the
Mayor this means a saving of $300,000
a year for the people of the city and
â¢approximately $40,000 to the municipal
government. As a matter of fact the
only saving Is to the local government.
Â«nd half of the amount is in accrued
bills.
The Mayor, in permitting himself to
be deceived into a bogus compromise
'by the crafty representatives of the
gas and electric light companies, has
simply delayed the people of the city
,from enjoying whatever dubious bene
fits the corporations were willing to
â prant four months ago. In February
Supervisor Aigeltinger offered a resolu
tion to take effect on March 1 and to
provide for a rate of $1 GO for gas. The
Mayor opposed the measure, and now,
lafter a great deal of noise and threaten
ing nonsense he accepts .it.
There was an air of great expectancy
around the Mayor's office yesterday
morning and afternoon. The minority
members were rushing in and out in
flurried activity. Representatives of
the gas and electric light companies
avere in conference with Mayor Phelan,
and all sorts of bogus compromises
â were suggested and discussed. Mayor
Phelan prepared a statement to the
majority members of the board pro
testing against their meeting without
"his sanction and declaring their former
meeting illegal. This the Mayor intend
ed to issue If the compromise with the
gas companies were not effected. The
subsequent developments of- the day
made it unnecessary to issue the doc
ument.
The representatives of the corpora
tions had their plan of action clearly
outlined before they went to the office
of the Mayor. They craftily asked
more than they wanted, and more than
they knew they could get, in order' to
make apparent concessions and secure
just what they wanted. Attorney
Pillsbury made two propositions, and
finally accepted -he alleged compro
mise as it was subsequently adopted by
the board.
While the discussion was taking place
In the Mayor's office the majority mem
bers of the Board of Supervisors were
in session In their assembly chamber.
They had Ignored the Mayor and were
transacting the ordinary business of the
board. Before any matters of import
ance had been considered the confer
ence in the Mayor's office ended with
the compromise, and the Mayor and
the five members of the board proceed
ed to the assembly chamber. The
Mayor had presented to every member
of the board an official call and then
took the presiding officers cna;r.
When the call was read Supervisor
Holland declared that the board was in
regular session, and he saw no reason
for such a document. He, therefore,
offered an objection to its presentation.
Mayor Phelan replied that he had is
sued the call simply to give legality to
the meeting of the board. "You may
have one opinion in reference to this
matter," he said, "and I may have an
other. I have done this simply to avoid
confusion and I ask Supervisor Holland
to withdraw his objection."
"I cannot do so," replied Holland, "as
I believe a call Is unnecessary.''
The Mayor asked the opinion of the
other members and Supervisor Aigel
tlnger declared that while he had no i
objection to the presence of the Mayor
he insisted that the meeting was regu
lar and no call was needed. Mayor
Phelan then decided that the call
should be spread upon the minutes and
thus become officially a part of the
meeting.
"Are we to consider then," asked Su
pervisor Aigeltinger, "that by permit
ting this proceeding we admit that
what we have already done is illegal?"
">int at all," was the Mayor's reply.
"I accept what you have done up to
this point and will not question it."
After the transaction of considerable
routine business Supervisor Perrault j
moved that the rules be suspended and j
the board listen. to a statement which j
the representative of the gas and elec- j
trie light companies wished to make, i
Attorney Bishop was then given an op
portunity to speak. He related how the |
Mayor and the minority had met in I
conference and had finally agreed upon
a compromise in reference to electric
and gas light rates. The compromise ;
was described in detail and when the j
attorney had finished Supervisor Deasy |
moved that it be accepted and adopted
by the board.
This gave Mayor Phelan the oppor
tunity to commit perhaps the most bril- i
liant mistake of his administration.
He suggested that it would be far bet
ter to have the terms of the compro- I
mise placed in the form of a written |
resolution. This was done by the at
torney for the gas and electric light
companies and was presented in the
following gem of crafty manipulation:
Resolved, That all existing contro
versies between the City, and County
of San Francisco and the San Fran-
Cisco Gas and Electric Company and
Pacific Gas Improvement Company
be and the same are hereby settled
upon the following basis of compro
mise, as agreed to by the said light
ing companies with his Honor the
Mayor, and this Board, to wit:
1. Bate for gas to consumers shall
be fixed at the rate of $1 50 per onÂ©
tnousand cubic feet of gas; this rate
to take effect July 1, 1899.
2. Bills accrued and to accrue for '
gas furnished public buildings shall
be paid for at the rate of $1 35 per j
one thousand cubic feet.
3. Bills accrued and to accrue for !
gas furnished for street lighting
shall be paid for at the rate of 10 !
cents per lamp per night, instead of
1 1 cents per light per night.
4. All electric lighting bills^ccrued
and. to accrue, whether for street I
lighting or public buildings, shall be I
paid with a reduction of 15 per cent
off the face of the bills rendered at
the contract price.
5. If new contracts are let for light- !
ing public buildings with gas, the ;
said lighting companies agree to bid ;
for the same at the rate of not to ex
ceed $1 35 per one thousand cubic
feet.
The foregoing resolution was adopted
by the board by a unanimous vote.
The gas and electric light corporations
had won their victory. They may now
go before Superior Judge Sea.well and
show that the city has rescinded its I
action in fixing a rate of $1 10 a thou
sand feet and has adopted a new rate,
at the same time and by its own action
abandoning its claim to a right to fix
gas rates once a year.
Upon motion of Supervisor Aigeltin
ger the following resolution fixing a
maximum rate and establishing stand
ards was also adopted:
Resolved, That the minimum stand
ard quality and illuminating power of
gas to be furnished by any person, com.
pany or. corporation to be used in the
city and county of San Francisco is
hereby fixed and established at seven
teen candles, and the maximum rate to
be charged and collected therefor from
consumers by any such person, com
pany or corporation is hereby fixed and
established at $1 50 per one thousand
cubic feet.
Secondâ All ordinances and resolu- I
tions and parts of ordinances and reso
lutions in conflict herewith are hereby
repealed.
Thirdâ resolution shall be in
force and take effect on the first day
of July, 1899.
The very Important question of gas
rates had been determined and the city
had lost through the inability of the
Mayor and the minority to see the plot
which had been hatched for them. Af
ter the meeting Mayor Pbelan gave an
outline of his position in the matter i
and claimed that the question of the I
$1 10 rate is properly before the courts.
The Mayor did not realize when he
made that remark that by the agree
ment, craftily framed by corporation
agents and accepted by the city, the ;
$1 10 rate had been officially aban
doned. His Honor is probably wiser to
day.
In his discussion of the events of the
session of the board, the Mayor also
described the Incidents of the various
conferences, and saw a victory In the
fact that the gas and electric light com
panies had at last, after bitter and I
continued contention, admitted the
right of the city to fix rates for gas
and electric lighting.
During the meeting of the Board of
Supervisors the problem of Btreet fran
chises was also . considered, but ; noth
ing of importance was done. By In
THE SAIST FKANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, JTXKE 13, 1899.
MAD STEED RUNS AMUCK
ON A CROWDED STREET
HEROIC ACTION OF JAMES GALLAGHER
EDESTRIANS on Market street
P^V were treated to a thrilling sight
I M yesterday afternoon. A power
\^T ful black mustang dashed up
j the thoroughfare, endangering
I the lives of passengers on the
" cable cars and sightseers In its
flight. But for the heroic action of
James Gallagher, a teamster employed
by Curtaz & Sons, who at the risk of
his life brought the animal to a stop,
pedestrians might have been killed and
great damage done to the show win
dows of the stores In that neighbor
hood.
About 3 o'clock William Beckmann,
who keeps a grocery store at the cor
ner of Sixth and Bryant streets, was
driving his spirited animal on Market
street. In the vehicle was a number
of boxes of potatoes. When close to
Grant avenue the horse took fright
and reared, snapping the shaft from
its fastenings. This caused the ani
mal to become further alarmed, and
with the bit in his teeth he shot up
Market street. The start was so sud-
vitation President Dohrmann of the
Merchants' Association addressed the
board in advocacy of the proposed
corkscrew and Post street franchise.
Representatives of the North Ontral
Improvement Club and of the Sixth
Streel Improvement Club also spoke,
and the board referred all franchises
back to the Street Committee for fur
ther consideration. The flouble track
franchise asked by the Southern Pa
cific Company will not be granted, as
its consideration was indefinitely post

Wr.s Not Murdered
The old man supposed to be A. P. Hen
derson, who died suddenly last Saturday
night at 635 Clay street, died of apoplexy
and was not murdered, as was believed.
An autopsy yesterday by Morgue Surgeon
Zabala established the fact that death
was due to natural causes.

who Mr. Phelps was. After fixing up
the machine they rode on to Los Gatos
and hack to the city. They were not
riding faster than eleven miles an
hour, and express regret at the result
of the accident.

HEALTH BOARD
WILL CAUCUS
ON PATRONAGE
Regular Session to
Follow Conference.
TALK OF A FIXED PROGRAMME
RUMORS OF A SLATE WITH LEON
DENNERY FOR SECRETARY.
Affairs at the San Jose Nor
mal School Are in a Fair
Way of Adjust
ment.
There will be a meeting of the new
Board of Health this forenoon. At places
where the politicians loiter ami discuss
patronage distribution the opinion is gen
erally expressed that Leon Dennery will
be. elected secretary of the board.
Last evening Drs. Barbat, Coffey and
Payne, newly-appointed members, were
asked if it was on the programme to se
lect Dennery for Feoretary.
Dr. Barbat replied: 'The subject will
probably be considered at a conference of
morniru before
the regular meeting. I do not understand
that there is an agreement to elect Den
mry. We have had hut one caucus since
our appointment and no decision has been
reached regarding the secretaryship. "
Dr. R. W. Payne in answer to The Call
inquiry said: "I do not know of any
agreement or understanding to elect Leon
Pennery secretary of the board. I know
that he is a candidate and believe that he
is confident of success."
Dr. W. B. Coffey replied: "I do not know
of any programme to elect Dennery sec
retary of the board. There may be some
agreement or understanding that he shall
b* chosen for the place, but I am not in
formed of it. Three members can elect.
Yes the Mayor has a vote, but 1 do not
know that he will express a choice. Mr.
Dennery is not the only candidate. Ihere
are others."
In view of the incident that law and
moral sentiment have been invoked to
suppress an indecent exhibition at the
California Theater, the members of the
Htalth Board may feel constrained to re
flect seriously before they accept a pro
gramme which contemplates the selection
oi: Dennery for secretary.
The members of the Board of Health
are reputable physicians in good standing.
The question may occur to them, Can -are
justify such an appointment to our fellow
members of the medical fraternity?
Physicians, as a rule, demand a good if
not a high standard of personal character
and intellectual qualification in men as
piring to public service, and they may ex
pect their fellow-members on the Health
Board to. recognize the standard.
Ir appointing Drs. Barbat, Coffey,
Pavne and Keeney to compose the Board
of 'Health the Governor was influenced
mainly by the Southern Pacific Company
and Dan" Burns. Gage believes thut he
was discovered by Burns and elected by
I-lerrin. and thotefoie holds ihat common
gratitude requires him to follow tho
course outlined by the Candelarian and
the corporation.
The appointments to the Board oi
Health are about the best of Gage's ad
ministration. The Call is conscious that
no great compliment to the board is con
veyed in this observation, but the fact
is obvious, however, that worse appoint
ments could have been made, and it is
therefore a bit surprising that the Gov
ernor did not make them.
While Gage is going down the line and
Inviting some rare specimens of un
worthiness to step forward, it is doubtful
if Dan Bruns and Herrin can elicit a
recommendation from him that Leon Den
nerv be appointed secretary of the Board
of Health.
The political strife at the San Jose Nor
mal School is In a fair way to be con
cluded. The directors have practically
agreed on a scheme that they declare will
end existing unpleasantness and further
the best interests of the s'thool.
On the 19th of August the new presi
dent, Dr. McNaughten. who was recently
elected, will present his report to the
board. According to the rules of the
school, the Board of Directors must eclef.c f .
teachers from nominations made by the
president. It is known that in his re
port President MrNaughten will recom
mend that Professor Childs and Professor
Randell each be placed at the head of an
important department in the Normal
School.
This plan will pacify both elements who
are .politically Interested in the school.
Professor Randell is now president, and
was appointed by Governor Budd four
rears ago in place <>f Professor Childs.
Each man has a strong following of
friends, and of course should either one
be placed at the head of the Institution
the other"s friends would be dissatisfied.
It was thought host to bring a new man
to control the institution and to place
both the ex-presidents in important posi
tions, as both are very competent men.
Tn order to do this the heads of two
of the departments will of course be
dropped. One of them will probably be
the head of the history department, but
the other is not yet decided upon. When
President .Rande'll assumed charge four
years ago it was regarded as significant
that his report did not recommend the
appointment of his predecessor, Childs. in
the history department, as was antici
pated, but such a recommendation will
probably be included in President Mc-
Naughten's report. M .
The new president is elected for one
year, and the directors will not make any
important changes among the other
teachers for several months until an op
portunity has been afforded the new
board of testing the value of the material
at present employed.

Edward O'Donnell, a laborer, fired two
Bhots at "Jerry" Murphy, a recent ar
rival from Boston, in a saloon at Sixth
and FolÃom streets Sunday night during
a dispute about the Jeffries-Fitzsommons
fight. He was arrested on a charge of
assault to murder and appeared for his

Treadwell yesterday. After hearing the
evidence the Judge held him to answer

DISCUSSED THE PRESS
IN ITS VARIOUS PHASES
DR. HAMMOND SAYS NEWSPA
PERS ARE A BLESSING.
Rev. John Hammond, D.D., of the
Methodist Church South. Alameda. read
a paper on "The Press 1 ' before the
Methodist Ministers' Union at their week
ly meeting yesterday morning. In part
he said:
Of onursp the press pxatrtcprntes and there is
no remedy hut to convert the reporters and the
Mitors. The Apostles wpn> the first reporters,
but unfortunately reporters are not Apostles.
but neither are all ministers.
iÂ»n the whole, there is perhaps no greater
blessings to our civilization than the. press The
newspaper if? an old institution. God was th*s
first e.iitor-the stars but "extras" posted upon
the great bulletin hoard of the night. What
is the sin but a great newsboy leaving a copy
of heaven's ereat daily at the doorway of
every island, telling us that there is no change
in the policy of the Heaven's administration
but that love still reigns''
The duty nf the press is to let the world
Know Itself; to paint a real, not an ideal world.
Ufesized pictures of the penthouse must b.
hung In sight of Nob Hill. The press is the
artist that must paint them: the press is the
telephone that must carry up th" thundi
the strike to the parlors of the capitalists and
br^ak his siesta with the cry of labor It is
thus wrongs will be righted, but they must be
first known.
During; thf> discussion which followed
the conclusion of I >r. Hammond's paper
Rev. W. B. Anderson, pastor of Bethle
hem A. M. E. church, took occasion to
rebuke the ministers for not having; con
demned the Georgia lynching?. He spoke
of their being solicitous about the suf
fering Armenians and the threo mission
aries who were killed in Africa. "My
people are burdened, heavily oppress. ,] "
ho said, "but by the help of God and the
preps they will yet he able to develop.
It is known that the pr<=ss had much to
do with the abolition of slavery: now it
has taken up the subject of lynch law
and what are we doing:? I am speaking
now in the cause of humanity. 1 think
we need touching; up. I have taken cour
age and am determined to speak out.
Some prpachers can't speak because they
are crooked."
The rebuke was mildly received.
